Universal Tasbīḥ: The Two Tongues of Glorification
Sanusi explains that all things glorify Allah, either by tongue of state or by tongue of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues that all creation glorifies Allah by two modes: tongue of state and tongue of speech.

Core Idea
All creation proclaims Allah's perfection through its very existence or through actual speech.

Sanusi begins by grounding universal glorification in the contingency of the world. He shows that every part of creation, by its inherent need and incapacity, points to its Creator. This passage introduces the two ways this glorification occurs: through the inherent state of things (tongue of state) and through actual verbal praise (tongue of speech). Understanding this helps reconcile the Qur'anic verse with rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on universal glorification
Carefully read the Arabic and translation, noting how Sanusi connects contingency to glorification.
Notice the phrase 'لسان الحال أو لسان المقال'.
2
Understand the two modes of glorification
Distinguish between glorification by tongue of state (inherent indication) and tongue of speech (actual verbal praise).
Tongue of state is a concept in Sufi and kalam literature.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does every part and attribute declare its need? Follow the logical chain from contingency to praise.
This reasoning is a common kalam argument for divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإنْ مِنْ شيءٍ إلا يُسبِّحُ بحمدِهِ ) يعني : لمَّا وجبَ الحدوث للعالم ؛ وهو كلُّ ما سوى الله جل وعلا ، ووجبَ عجزُ عموماً عنِ التأثيرِ في شيءٍ ، أي شيءٍ كانَ ، وكانَتِ الدلالةُ جميعه على ذلكَ مِنْ جهةِ فطرتِهِ .. صارَ كلُّ جزءٍ مِنْ أجزائِهِ وكلُّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاته ينبئ بعظيم افتقاره إلى مبدع له غاية الكمال ، ويُثني على ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ وصفاتِهِ الكاملة بلسان الحال أو بلسان المقال ، ويعترفُ بالعجزِ عن الإدراك والشكرِ لِمَنْ تَحيَّرَتِ العقولُ في كُنْهِ جَلالِهِ ، وتَنَزَّهَ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ مِنْ جميعِ ما يُتخيَّلُ مثال ، تباركَ اللهُ ربُّ العالمينَ . [ لا يُشترط في الحياة والعلم وغيرِهما بنية مخصوصة عند أهل السنَّةِ ] وقيل : إِنَّ التسبيحَ في الآية على ظاهره في جميع الموجوداتِ ؛ لا يُشترط في الحياةِ والعلم وغيرِهما مِنَ الصفاتِ بنيةٌ مخصوصةٌ عندَ أهل السنة (٢) . (٢) قوله : ( على ظاهره ) أي : من كونه بلسان المقال ؛ بأن تقول : سبحان الله وبحمده ، وقوله : ( إذ لا يشترط .. ) فلا مانع من قيام الحياة والعلم وباقي الصفات التي يتوقف عليها الفعلُ .. بالأحجار وغيرها من الجمادات .
His saying: (And there is not a thing except that it glorifies His praise) means: when the temporality of the world was necessitated—and it is everything besides Allah Almighty—and its universal incapacity for effecting anything, any thing whatsoever, and the indication of all of it to that from the aspect of its innate disposition... every part of its parts and every attribute of its attributes becomes announcing its immense need for an Originator of utmost perfection, and praising His supreme Essence and perfect attributes by the tongue of state or the tongue of speech, and confessing inability to comprehend and thank Him before Whom intellects are bewildered at the depth of His majesty, and He is exalted above having any example from all that can be imagined, blessed be Allah, Lord of the worlds. [Life and knowledge and other attributes do not require a specific intention according to Ahl al-Sunnah] And it is said: Indeed the glorification in the verse is taken literally in all existents; life and knowledge and other attributes do not require a specific intention according to Ahl al-Sunnah. (2) His saying: (literally) means: that it is by the tongue of speech; you say: subhan Allah wa bihamdihi, and his saying: (since it is not conditional...) so there is no obstacle to life, knowledge, and the rest of the attributes upon which action depends subsisting in stones and other inanimate objects.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the Qur'anic verse that all things glorify God. The author reasons that since the world is contingent and all its parts are incapable of effecting anything, each part and attribute declares its need for a perfect Creator. This glorification is either by the tongue of state (ḥāl) or by the tongue of speech (maqāl). The passage then addresses a theological point: according to Ahl al-Sunnah, the literal glorification of inanimate objects does not require that they possess life, knowledge, or a specific intention. The footnote clarifies that 'literally' here means actual speech, and that life and knowledge can in principle be accidentally present in stones and inanimate objects (as a divine act), so there is no barrier to them performing tasbīḥ with actual speech.

Key Terms
تسبيح
لسان الحال
لسان المقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only through inherent nature
B) Only through speech

D) Only through angel intermediaries

Key Takeaway
All creation, by its very existence and attributes, proclaims Allah's perfection in two ways: through its dependent state and through actual verbal praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Conditions for Tasbīḥ: Life and Knowledge Not Required
Sanusi clarifies that according to Ahl al-Sunnah, the literal glorification of inanimate objects does not require them to have life or knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah position that the literal glorification of all things does not require life, knowledge, or a specific intention.

Core Idea
Literal tasbīḥ of inanimate objects is possible without intrinsic life or intention.

After establishing universal glorification, Sanusi addresses a critical question: Do the things that glorify Allah need to possess life and knowledge? The Ahl al-Sunnah answer is no. This lesson unpacks that position, clarifying that the verse is taken literally and that the lack of conditions does not contradict Allah's power to grant life accidentally. This preserves the universality of the verse while avoiding anthropomorphic pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ahl al-Sunnah position
The passage clearly states that life, knowledge, and a specific intention are not required for universal tasbīḥ.
This is in response to those who might think only living beings can praise.
2
Understand the 'literal' interpretation
The footnote explains that 'literally' means actual speech, i.e., tongue of speech, and that life can be accidentally present in stones.
This reinforces that inanimate objects can perform verbal tasbīḥ.
3
Consider the theological implications
This position upholds the apparent meaning of the verse without attributing necessary life to all things.
It maintains divine omnipotence: Allah can grant life wherever He wills.

This glorification is either by the tongue of state (ḥāl) or by the tongue of speech (maqāl). The passage then addresses a theological point: according to Ahl al-Sunnah, the literal glorification of inanimate objects does not require that they possess life, knowledge, or a specific intention. The footnote clarifies that 'literally' here means actual speech, and that life and knowledge can in principle be accidentally present in stones and inanimate objects (as a divine act), so there is no barrier to them performing tasbīḥ with actual speech.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
بنية مخصوصة
لسان المقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that only living beings can literally praise Allah, and that inanimate objects cannot perform actual tasbīḥ.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that inanimate objects can literally glorify Allah without requiring intrinsic life or knowledge, and Allah can grant them life accidentally.

Checkpoint
What condition does Ahl al-Sunnah set for the literal glorification of all existents?
A) They must possess life and knowledge
B) They must have a specific intention
C) They do not require life, knowledge, or specific intention
D) Only humans and angels can do it

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah maintain that the literal glorification of all things does not depend on them having life, knowledge, or intention, preserving the verse's universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every rock, tree, and speck of dust around you is at this very moment uttering 'Subhan Allah' with actual words? If you really convinced yourself of this, how would your attitude toward creation change?
?
You have no problem accepting that Allah can give speech to inanimate objects—you've believed in the miracles of the prophets. But do you live as if He is constantly speaking through the world around you, or do you treat nature as mute and meaningless?
?
If the stones glorify Allah without intention, what is your excuse for failing to glorify Him with full intention and awareness? What would it take for you to join that cosmic chorus today?

Tasbih and the Attributes of Life and Knowledge
Sanusi discusses whether the glorification of inanimate objects requires life and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna's position that the glorification of all creatures, including inanimate objects, does not require a specific intention of life and knowledge.

Core Idea
Glorification by inanimate objects does not depend on life and knowledge—it is a direct act of Allah's power.

What does it mean when the Qur'an says everything glorifies Allah? Does a stone or a tree need to be alive and aware to do so? In this passage, Imam Sanusi clarifies the stance of Ahl al-Sunna that such glorification does not require a specific intention of life and knowledge. The following lines explore this subtle but important theological nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement
Read the first part of the passage, which states the general position of Ahl al-Sunna that life and knowledge are not conditioned upon a specific intention for tasbih.
Notice the phrase 'بنية مخصوصة' (specific intention).
2
Examine the second opinion
The passage then mentions an opinion that the tasbih in the verse is upon its apparent meaning, i.e., literal speech. Recognize that this view aligns with the Sunni stance.
3
Understand the footnote clarification
The footnote explains that 'apparent meaning' means 'by the tongue of speech' (bi-lisan al-maqal), such as saying 'Subhan Allah'. It also clarifies that not conditioning life and knowledge removes any barrier to inanimate objects having these attributes for the action of glorification.
This shows how the Sunni position is consistent with the literal interpretation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this teaching emphasizes Allah's absolute power to grant speech and attributes to any creation, regardless of its apparent nature.
This connects to the broader Sunni theology of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ لا يُشترط في الحياة والعلم وغيرِهما بنية مخصوصة عند أهل السنَّةِ ] وقيل : إِنَّ التسبيحَ في الآية على ظاهره في جميع الموجوداتِ ؛ لا يُشترط في الحياةِ والعلم وغيرِهما مِنَ الصفاتِ بنيةٌ مخصوصةٌ عندَ أهل السنة (٢) . And his saying: (since it is not conditioned) so there is no prevention of the subsistence of life and knowledge and the rest of the attributes upon which the action depends in stones and other inanimate objects.

This passage begins with a statement that life and knowledge and other attributes are not conditioned upon a specific intention according to the Ahl al-Sunna when it comes to the glorification of all things. Then it mentions that some say the tasbih in the verse is upon its apparent meaning, i.e., actual speech. The footnote clarifies that the apparent meaning means speaking, as in saying 'Subhan Allah'. It also explains that not conditioning life and knowledge means there is no barrier to stones and inanimate objects having life and knowledge on which the action of glorification depends. This is because the Ahl al-Sunna do not require a specific intention.

Answering the Objection: Endless Paradise vs. Beginningless Accidents
Sanusi answers the objection that endless accidents in paradise face the same impossibility as beginningless ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the endless accidents of paradise do not fall under the same impossibility as beginningless accidents, due to the equivocal meaning of 'no end'.

Core Idea
Endless can mean no beginning or no end; only the first is impossible.

The atheists try to trap the theologian by claiming that if beginningless accidents are impossible, then endless future accidents in paradise are also impossible. But this objection relies on a confusion. Sanusi clarifies the two meanings of 'accidents with no end' and shows that only beginningless accidents are impossible, not endless future ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage carefully to understand the atheists' objection and Sanusi's response. Note the flow of the argument.
The objection is a common one; the answer hinges on a distinction.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where Sanusi explains the two meanings of 'accidents with no end'. One is about the beginning, the other about the end.
Look for the Arabic phrases 'لا نهاية لها بحسَبِ المبدأ' and 'لا نهاية لها بحسَبِ الآخر'.
3
Understand which meaning is impossible
Sanusi says the impossibility proofs apply only to the first meaning (beginningless). Why? Because it would require completing an infinite past.
The second meaning (endless future) does not involve an infinite past.
4
Apply to paradise's bliss
Paradise's accidents have a beginning but no end, so they are of the second type. Also, those that have occurred so far are finite.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أورد الملاحدة على ما منعناهُ مِنْ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها سؤالاً ; فقالوا : ما ألزمتُمونا من استحالة وجودِ حوادث لا نهاية لها يلزمكم مثله في نعيم الجنةِ ; إذْ قلتُم : إنَّ حوادث نعيمها ومُتجدّدات أفراحها وسرورها لا نهاية له ; وجوابُهُ : أَنْ يُقالَ لهم : لبَّسْتُم بلفظ مشترك ; وهو لفظ ( حوادث لا نهاية لها ) ، فإِنَّهُ يُطلَقُ على وجهين : بمعنى : لا نهاية لها بحسَبِ المبدأ ; أي : حوادثُ لا أَوَّلَ لها، وبمعنى : لا نهاية لها بحسَبِ الآخرِ ; أي : حوادث لا آخرَ لها ، والذي قلتُم به ورددناه الأوَّلُ وفيهِ وُجِدَتْ أدلَّةُ الاستحالةِ ; مِنَ الجمع بين الفراغ وعدم النهاية المتناقضين وغير ذلكَ ، وانعدم فيه دليلُ الجواز . وأما ما قلناه في نعيم الجنةِ مِنَ الحوادثِ : فهو مِنَ القسم الثاني ; أي : الحوادث التي فيها لا آخِرَ لها ; بمعنى : أنَّها لا تنقطع أبداً حتى لا يتجدَّدُ بعدها شيء . وأمَّا كلُّ ما وجدنا منها فيما مضى إلى زمانِ الحالِ . . فهو متناه له
And the atheists raised an objection against what we prevented of accidents with no first; they said: What you have required of us regarding the impossibility of the existence of accidents with no end is also required of you in the pleasures of paradise, since you said that the accidents of its bliss and the renewed things of its joys and delights have no end. And its answer: That it be said to them: You have confused by an ambiguous expression, which is the expression 'accidents with no end'. For it is used in two senses: meaning no end with respect to the beginning, i.e., accidents with no first; and meaning no end with respect to the other, i.e., accidents with no last. That which you raised and we rejected is the first, and in it the proofs of impossibility are found—from the combination of completion and the absence of end, which are contradictory, and other things—and the proof of permissibility is absent therein. As for what we said about the accidents in the bliss of paradise, it is of the second type; i.e., accidents that have no last; meaning: they never cease so that nothing after them would be renewed. And as for everything that we have found of them in the past up to the present time, it is finite for it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The atheists (malāḥida) object: You (the theologians) claim that beginningless accidents are impossible, but you also hold that the bliss of paradise has endless accidents. Why isn't the same impossibility present there? The answer is a clarification of equivocation (mushtarak). The phrase 'accidents with no end' has two meanings: (1) with respect to the beginning—meaning beginningless, and (2) with respect to the other end—meaning endless future. The impossibility applies only to the first meaning (beginningless), because it would require the completion of an infinite past, which is contradictory. The endless future accidents of paradise are of the second type: they have no last but they do have a beginning (the start of paradise), so they are not subject to the same argument. Furthermore, those endless future accidents that have already occurred from the beginning of paradise up to the present are finite, because only a finite amount of time has passed. Thus there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
ملاحدة (atheists)
حوادث لا نهاية لها (accidents with no end)
لفظ مشترك (ambiguous expression)
مبدأ (beginning)
آخر (end)
نعيم الجنة (bliss of paradise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The endless accidents of paradise are the same as beginningless accidents, so they should also be impossible.
Correction: Endless can refer to no beginning or no end; only no beginning is impossible because it would require an infinite past to be completed. Paradise's bliss has a beginning and only goes forward infinitely, so it is not impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that endless accidents in paradise are not impossible?
A) Because paradise is non-physical and thus not subject to logical laws.
B) Because 'endless' in this context means no last, not no beginning, so no infinite past needs to be completed.
C) Because the accidents of paradise are not real accidents but only metaphors.
D) Because the number of accidents in paradise is finite.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is between beginningless (impossible) and endless future (possible), and the objection fails due to equivocation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth of faith because you misunderstood a term? Are there any Islamic beliefs you have felt uneasy about because of a similar confusion? Will you take time to investigate the actual meanings before rejecting?
?
The atheists in the passage used an ambiguous expression to create doubt. Are there areas in your own understanding of Islam where you have allowed ambiguous terms from skeptics to shake your certainty? What steps will you take to clarify such terms?
?
The scholars like Sanusi took great care to resolve objections with precision. Do you give the same effort to understand your faith deeply, or do you rely on superficial explanations? Will you commit to studying one subtle point of aqeedah this week?

Proof of Endless Future from Divine Attributes
Sanusi proves endless future possibility via divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that endless future events are possible because God's power and will must attach to every possible thing, and that an end to events would imply impotence.

Core Idea
An endless future is possible; a beginningless past is not.

Sanusi now presents the rational foundation for the possibility of an endless series of events. He first refutes any previous objections, then builds his proof on the universal scope of God's power and will: if events had to have a last, that would limit God, which is impossible. He also draws a sharp contrast with beginningless events, which involve an actual infinite and are inherently impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening refutation
The passage begins by stating that the proofs of impossibility for endless events have been refuted. Notice that Sanusi is building on previous discussions.
This sets the stage for the positive proof.
2
Identify the proof from divine attributes
The core proof: God's power and will are general—they attach to every possible thing. If events had to end, that would mean God cannot create after a certain point, implying impotence. Since God is omnipotent, that cannot be.
The proof does not come from scripture but from rational necessity.
3
Distinguish beginningless from endless
Sanusi then states that beginningless events are of the kind that is impossible—they are not objects of power and will. This sets up the next lesson's examples.
Understand the asymmetry: ending is impossible because it limits divine power; beginningless is impossible because it involves an actual infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ظهر انتفاء أدلة الاستحالة فيما ادعيناه من ثبوت حوادث لا آخر لها . وأما دليل جوازه : فما تقرر وسيأتي برهانه من وجوب العموم في تعلق قدرته جل وعلا وإرادته بكل ممكن ، وكذا سائر صفاته فيما تتعلق به ، فلو وجب أن يكون للحوادث آخر للزم عجز القدرة والإرادة ممكنة ضرورة ، وأما حوادث لا أول لها فهي عن أمثال ما وقع ، وهي من المحال الذي ليس متعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة . • وقد ضرب أئمتنا لما ادعوه من حوادث لا أول لها ، ولما ادعيناه من حوادث لا آخر لها .. مثالين يستبين بهما أمر الاستحالة فيما ادعوه ، وأمر الجواز فيما ادعيناه؛ فمثلوا الأول : بملتزم قال : لا أعطي فلاناً في اليوم الفلاني درهماً حتى أعطيه درهماً قبله ولا أعطيه درهماً قبله حتى أعطيه درهماً قبله. وهكذا لا إلى أول، فمن المعلوم ضرورة أن إعطاءه الدرهم الموعود به في اليوم الفلاني محال؛ لتوقفه على محال، وهو فراغ ما لا نهاية له بالإعطاء شيئاً بعد شيء . ولا ريب أن ما ادعوه من حوادث لا أول لها مطابق لهذا المثال؛ فإن إعطاء الفاعل للفلك مثلاً الحركة في زماننا هذا أو في غيره من الأزمان المعينة متوقف على إعطائه قبله من الحركات شيئاً بعد شيء مما لا نهاية له؛ فالحركة للفلك في الزمان المعين نظير الدرهم الموعود به في الزمان المخصوص، والحركات التي لا تتناهى قبلها نظير الدراهم التي لا تتناهى قبل ذلك الدرهم، فيكون وجود الحركة للفلك في هذا الزمان مثلاً مستحيلاً، كما استحال وجود الدرهم الموعود به في الزمان المعين للشخص، وكذا يلزم أن يكون وجودنا في هذا الزمان ووجود سائر الحيوانات والزروع مستحيلاً؛ لتوقفنا على وجود آباء قبلنا لا نهاية لهم، وتوقف الزروع على بذور قبلها لا نهاية لها ولا خبر في فضيحتهم كالعيان. ومثال ما ادعيناه نحن في نعيم الجنة : ما لو قال الملتزم : لا أعطي فلاناً درهماً في زمن إلا وأعطيه درهماً بعده... وهكذا لا إلى آخرٍ فهذا لا ريب لعاقل في جوازه؛ إذ حاصله : التزام الملتزم عدم قطع العطاء بعد ابتدائه، فإذا كان ممن لا يعرض لمثله خلف في وعده، ولا موت لذاته، ولا عجز يمنع نفوذ قدرته وإرادته .. فإنّا نقطع بوقوع ذلك منه أبداً ونؤمن به، وليس ذلك إلا الله مولانا جل وعلا . فهذا المثال لا تخفى مطابقته لما ادعيناه في نعيم الجنة للمؤمنين، ولا لما ندّعيه في عذاب جهنم للفلاسفة القائلين بقدم العالم وأضرابهم من الطبائعيين وسائر الكافرين، نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا في الدنيا والآخرة من حزبه المفلحين، الذين لا خوف عليهم ولا هم يحزنون، آمين يا رب العالمين.
So the proofs of impossibility have been refuted regarding what we claimed of the existence of events having no last. As for the proof of its permissibility: it is what has been established and whose proof will come of the necessity of generality in the attachment of His power (qudratihi), exalted and majestic, and His will (irādatihi) to every possible thing, and likewise the rest of His attributes in what they are attached to. For if it were necessary that events have a last, then impotence of power and will would necessarily follow, whereas both are possible. As for events having no first, they are of the type of what has occurred, and they are among the impossible which is not an object of power and will. Our imams gave for what they claimed of events having no first, and for what we claimed of events having no last, two examples by which the matter of impossibility in what they claimed and the matter of permissibility in what we claimed become clear. They exemplified the first by a obligator who said: 'I will not give Zayd a dirham on such-and-such a day until I give him a dirham before it, and I will not give him a dirham before it until I give him a dirham before it,' and so on without a first. It is known by necessity that his giving the promised dirham on that specific day is impossible, because it is dependent on an impossibility, namely, emptying what has no end by giving one thing after another. There is no doubt that what they claimed of events having no first corresponds to this example; for the agent's giving the sphere, for example, motion in this time or in other specific times is dependent on His giving before it of motions one after another which have no end. So the motion of the sphere at the specific time is analogous to the promised dirham at the specific time, and the motions that are infinite before it are analogous to the dirhams that are infinite before that dirham. Thus the existence of motion for the sphere at this time, for example, becomes impossible, just as the existence of the promised dirham at the specific time for the person became impossible. Likewise, it follows that our existence in this time and the existence of all animals and plants would be impossible, because we depend on the existence of ancestors before us having no end, and plants depend on seeds before them having no end. And there is no news of their disgrace like visible evidence. And the example of what we claim concerning the bliss of Paradise is: if the obligator said, 'I will not give Zayd a dirham in any time except that I give him a dirham after it,' and so on without a last. This, no rational person doubts its permissibility, because its essence is the obligator's commitment not to cut off giving after its beginning. So if he is someone for whom there is no breaking of his promise, nor death of his essence, nor impotence preventing the effectiveness of his power and will, then we are certain of its occurrence from him forever and we believe in it. And that is none other than Allah, our Lord, exalted and majestic. This example's correspondence to what we claim concerning the bliss of Paradise for the believers is not hidden, nor to what we claim concerning the torment of Hell for the philosophers who affirm the eternity of the world and their like among the naturalists and all the disbelievers. We ask Him, exalted, to make us in this world and the Hereafter among His successful party, upon whom no fear shall come nor shall they grieve. Amen, O Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a central argument in the Ash'ari defense of the possibility of endless future events (like eternal bliss or punishment) and the impossibility of beginningless past events (like an eternal world). The author begins by stating that the objections to our claim of events with no last have been refuted. Then he gives the proof for the permissibility of endless events: God's power and will must be general, attaching to every possible thing. If events had to have a last, that would imply impotence. However, beginningless events are impossible because they involve an actual infinite regress, which is something impossible in itself and not an object of power. To clarify, the imams provide two examples. For the claim of beginningless events (their opponents' view), they give the example of a person who promises to give a dirham on a specific day conditional on giving a dirham before it, and that before that, etc., ad infinitum. This is known to be impossible because it requires completing an infinite series. Similarly, if the world had an infinite past, then present motion, our existence, etc., would depend on an infinite chain, which is impossible. For our claim of endless events (like heaven and hell), they give the example of someone who commits to giving a dirham in every time and after it, without end. This is rationally permissible because it only involves not cutting off after beginning. If the giver is eternal, truthful, and omnipotent, then this endless giving is certain. This applies directly to God's eternal reward and punishment. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
ممكن
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both an endless future and a beginningless past are equally impossible or equally possible.
Correction: Sanusi argues they are fundamentally different: an endless future only requires non-termination after a start (possible under infinite power), while a beginningless past requires traversal of an actual infinite (impossible).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would necessarily follow if events had to have a last?
A) God's will would cease.
B) Impotence of power and will.
C) All things would become possible.
D) The universe would end.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of endless future events is rooted in the universal scope of God's power and will, whereas a beginningless past is impossible due to the infinite regress it requires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's power and will extend to every possible thing without end, how would that change your attitude toward your own unanswered prayers? Will you continue to ask with confidence even if you have asked a thousand times? What stops you from doing that right now?
?
The passage teaches that a beginningless past is impossible, while an endless future is possible. How does this affect your sense of personal accountability? If your life has no ultimate end, what are you building that will matter forever? What are you wasting time on that will not?
?
God's power and will are not limited. But you often feel powerless in specific areas of your life. Which area do you most need to entrust to His general and endless power today? What is one concrete step you will take to act on that trust?

The Two Dirham Examples: Infinite Past vs. Infinite Future
Sanusi presents two examples for beginningless (impossible) and endless (possible).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses two analogous dirham examples to demonstrate the impossibility of a beginningless past (actual infinite) and the possibility of an endless future (non-termination), applying them to the world's past and the afterlife.

Core Idea
You can promise forever, but you can't promise before the beginning.

After establishing the rational framework, Sanusi now brings the arguments to life with two concrete examples. The first example mirrors the claim of an eternal world: a promise that depends on an infinite chain of prior conditions, leading to impossibility. The second mirrors the eternal bliss or punishment: a promise to give without end, which is perfectly rational if the giver is eternal and omnipotent. These examples make the abstract distinction vivid and directly applicable to theology.

Guided Path
1
Study the first example (beginningless)
Sanusi gives the example of a person who says: 'I will give a dirham on a specific day only after I give a dirham before it, and before that, etc., without a first.' This is clearly impossible because it requires traversing an infinite series to reach the promised day.
This example mirrors the claim of an eternal world with an infinite past.
2
Apply the example to the world's past
The author then applies: if the world had an infinite past, then present motion, our existence, etc., would depend on an infinite chain of prior events, making them impossible. This shows the absurdity of a beginningless past.
Notice the logical mapping: the promised dirham corresponds to today's motion; the infinite prior dirhams correspond to infinite past motions.
3
Study the second example (endless future)
The second example: a person says 'I will give a dirham in every time and after it, without end.' This is rationally permissible because it only means not cutting off after starting. If the giver is truthful, immortal, and omnipotent, then it is certain to happen.
This directly applies to God's promise of eternal reward and punishment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ظهر انتفاء أدلة الاستحالة فيما ادعيناه من ثبوت حوادث لا آخر لها . وأما دليل جوازه : فما تقرر وسيأتي برهانه من وجوب العموم في تعلق قدرته جل وعلا وإرادته بكل ممكن ، وكذا سائر صفاته فيما تتعلق به ، فلو وجب أن يكون للحوادث آخر للزم عجز القدرة والإرادة ممكنة ضرورة ، وأما حوادث لا أول لها فهي عن أمثال ما وقع ، وهي من المحال الذي ليس متعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة . • وقد ضرب أئمتنا لما ادعوه من حوادث لا أول لها ، ولما ادعيناه من حوادث لا آخر لها .. مثالين يستبين بهما أمر الاستحالة فيما ادعوه ، وأمر الجواز فيما ادعيناه؛ فمثلوا الأول : بملتزم قال : لا أعطي فلاناً في اليوم الفلاني درهماً حتى أعطيه درهماً قبله ولا أعطيه درهماً قبله حتى أعطيه درهماً قبله. وهكذا لا إلى أول، فمن المعلوم ضرورة أن إعطاءه الدرهم الموعود به في اليوم الفلاني محال؛ لتوقفه على محال، وهو فراغ ما لا نهاية له بالإعطاء شيئاً بعد شيء . ولا ريب أن ما ادعوه من حوادث لا أول لها مطابق لهذا المثال؛ فإن إعطاء الفاعل للفلك مثلاً الحركة في زماننا هذا أو في غيره من الأزمان المعينة متوقف على إعطائه قبله من الحركات شيئاً بعد شيء مما لا نهاية له؛ فالحركة للفلك في الزمان المعين نظير الدرهم الموعود به في الزمان المخصوص، والحركات التي لا تتناهى قبلها نظير الدراهم التي لا تتناهى قبل ذلك الدرهم، فيكون وجود الحركة للفلك في هذا الزمان مثلاً مستحيلاً، كما استحال وجود الدرهم الموعود به في الزمان المعين للشخص، وكذا يلزم أن يكون وجودنا في هذا الزمان ووجود سائر الحيوانات والزروع مستحيلاً؛ لتوقفنا على وجود آباء قبلنا لا نهاية لهم، وتوقف الزروع على بذور قبلها لا نهاية لها ولا خبر في فضيحتهم كالعيان. ومثال ما ادعيناه نحن في نعيم الجنة : ما لو قال الملتزم : لا أعطي فلاناً درهماً في زمن إلا وأعطيه درهماً بعده... وهكذا لا إلى آخرٍ فهذا لا ريب لعاقل في جوازه؛ إذ حاصله : التزام الملتزم عدم قطع العطاء بعد ابتدائه، فإذا كان ممن لا يعرض لمثله خلف في وعده، ولا موت لذاته، ولا عجز يمنع نفوذ قدرته وإرادته .. فإنّا نقطع بوقوع ذلك منه أبداً ونؤمن به، وليس ذلك إلا الله مولانا جل وعلا . فهذا المثال لا تخفى مطابقته لما ادعيناه في نعيم الجنة للمؤمنين، ولا لما ندّعيه في عذاب جهنم للفلاسفة القائلين بقدم العالم وأضرابهم من الطبائعيين وسائر الكافرين، نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا في الدنيا والآخرة من حزبه المفلحين، الذين لا خوف عليهم ولا هم يحزنون، آمين يا رب العالمين.
So the proofs of impossibility have been refuted regarding what we claimed of the existence of events having no last. As for the proof of its permissibility: it is what has been established and whose proof will come of the necessity of generality in the attachment of His power (qudratihi), exalted and majestic, and His will (irādatihi) to every possible thing, and likewise the rest of His attributes in what they are attached to. For if it were necessary that events have a last, then impotence of power and will would necessarily follow, whereas both are possible. As for events having no first, they are of the type of what has occurred, and they are among the impossible which is not an object of power and will. Our imams gave for what they claimed of events having no first, and for what we claimed of events having no last, two examples by which the matter of impossibility in what they claimed and the matter of permissibility in what we claimed become clear. They exemplified the first by a obligator who said: 'I will not give Zayd a dirham on such-and-such a day until I give him a dirham before it, and I will not give him a dirham before it until I give him a dirham before it,' and so on without a first. It is known by necessity that his giving the promised dirham on that specific day is impossible, because it is dependent on an impossibility, namely, emptying what has no end by giving one thing after another. There is no doubt that what they claimed of events having no first corresponds to this example; for the agent's giving the sphere, for example, motion in this time or in other specific times is dependent on His giving before it of motions one after another which have no end. So the motion of the sphere at the specific time is analogous to the promised dirham at the specific time, and the motions that are infinite before it are analogous to the dirhams that are infinite before that dirham. Thus the existence of motion for the sphere at this time, for example, becomes impossible, just as the existence of the promised dirham at the specific time for the person became impossible. Likewise, it follows that our existence in this time and the existence of all animals and plants would be impossible, because we depend on the existence of ancestors before us having no end, and plants depend on seeds before them having no end. And there is no news of their disgrace like visible evidence. And the example of what we claim concerning the bliss of Paradise is: if the obligator said, 'I will not give Zayd a dirham in any time except that I give him a dirham after it,' and so on without a last. This, no rational person doubts its permissibility, because its essence is the obligator's commitment not to cut off giving after its beginning. So if he is someone for whom there is no breaking of his promise, nor death of his essence, nor impotence preventing the effectiveness of his power and will, then we are certain of its occurrence from him forever and we believe in it. And that is none other than Allah, our Lord, exalted and majestic. This example's correspondence to what we claim concerning the bliss of Paradise for the believers is not hidden, nor to what we claim concerning the torment of Hell for the philosophers who affirm the eternity of the world and their like among the naturalists and all the disbelievers. We ask Him, exalted, to make us in this world and the Hereafter among His successful party, upon whom no fear shall come nor shall they grieve. Amen, O Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a central argument in the Ash'ari defense of the possibility of endless future events (like eternal bliss or punishment) and the impossibility of beginningless past events (like an eternal world). The author begins by stating that the objections to our claim of events with no last have been refuted. Then he gives the proof for the permissibility of endless events: God's power and will must be general, attaching to every possible thing. If events had to have a last, that would imply impotence. However, beginningless events are impossible because they involve an actual infinite regress, which is something impossible in itself and not an object of power. To clarify, the imams provide two examples. For the claim of beginningless events (their opponents' view), they give the example of a person who promises to give a dirham on a specific day conditional on giving a dirham before it, and that before that, etc., ad infinitum. This is known to be impossible because it requires completing an infinite series. Similarly, if the world had an infinite past, then present motion, our existence, etc., would depend on an infinite chain, which is impossible. For our claim of endless events (like heaven and hell), they give the example of someone who commits to giving a dirham in every time and after it, without end. This is rationally permissible because it only involves not cutting off after beginning. If the giver is eternal, truthful, and omnipotent, then this endless giving is certain. This applies directly to God's eternal reward and punishment. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها
حوادث لا آخر لها
التزام
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two examples are symmetric and both impossible or both possible.
Correction: The first involves an actual infinite regress (impossible), the second involves an unbounded future (possible under an eternal agent).

Checkpoint
In the first example, why is the promised dirham on the specific day impossible?
A) Because the man might break his promise.
B) Because it depends on completing an infinite series of prior dirhams.
C) Because the specific day never arrives.
D) Because the man lacks sufficient money.

Key Takeaway
An infinite past is impossible because it requires completing an actual infinite series to reach the present; an endless future is possible because it only requires non-termination after a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world had a beginning, that means your existence is not accidental; you were created for a purpose. Does your daily life reflect that you truly believe you are here by God's design and for His worship? What would change if you lived today with that certainty?
?
The second example shows that God's promise of eternal bliss or punishment is certain because He is truthful, immortal, and omnipotent. If you were to stand before Him tonight, which promise would you inherit? What one concrete action can you take right now to ensure it is the promise of Paradise?
?
The first example reveals that without God as the first cause, nothing would exist—not even you. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you consciously acknowledge your dependence on God in every breath? Commit to a specific way you will remember your need for Him today.

Example of Infinite Future Reward and Punishment
Sanusi illustrates the possibility of eternal bliss and punishment using a promise of endless dirhams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a simple example to demonstrate that an infinite future series of rewards or punishments is rationally possible when made by an eternal, truthful, and all-powerful being.

Core Idea
An endless promise is possible if the promiser is eternal and truthful.

Sanusi provides a concrete example to make the abstract concept of eternal punishment and reward more accessible. By imagining a person who promises to give a dirham at every successive moment without end, he shows that such an infinite series is not inherently impossible. The key is the nature of the one making the promise. This passage invites us to reflect on the attributes of Allah that make His promises certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the example of the dirham promise
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure of the promise: 'I will not give a dirham at a time without giving another after it, ad infinitum.'
2
Identify the conditions for possibility
Sanusi lists three conditions for the promise to be possible: no breaking of promise, no death, no incapacity. Identify these in the text.
These conditions point to the attributes of Allah.
3
Relate the example to eternal reward and punishment
See how Sanusi applies this example to Paradise for believers and Hell for disbelievers. The same logic applies because Allah possesses the necessary attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument was important for refuting philosophers who denied the eternity of reward and punishment.
This connects to the broader theological context.

ومثال ما ادعيناه نحنُ في نعيم الجنةِ : ما لو قالَ المُلتِزِمُ : لا أعطي فلاناً درهماً في زمن إلا وأعطيه درهماً بعده... وهكذا لا إلى آخرٍ فهذا لا ريب لعاقل في جوازه ؛ إذْ حاصله : التزامُ المُلتزم عدم قطع العطاء بعد ابتدائِهِ ، فإذا كانَ ممَّنْ لا يَعرِضُ لمثلِهِ خُلْفٌ في وعدِهِ ، ولا موت لذاتِهِ ، ولا عجز يمنعُ نفوذ قدرتِهِ وإرادته .. فإنَّا نقطع بوقوع ذلك منه أبداً ونؤمنُ بهِ ، وليس ذلكَ إلا الله مولانا جل وعلا . فهذا المثال لا تخفى مطابقته لما ادعيناه في نعيم الجنة للمؤمنينَ ، ولا لما ندَّعيه في عذابِ جهنَّمَ للفلاسفة القائلين بقدم العالم وأضرابهم مِنَ الطبائعيين وسائر الكافرينَ ، نسأله سبحانَهُ أَنَّ يجعلنا في الدنيا والآخرةِ مِنْ حزبِهِ المفلحين ، الذين لا خوف عليهم ولا هم يحزنون ، آمين يا ربَّ العالمينَ
An example of what we claim regarding the bliss of Paradise is: if a person commits himself by saying, 'I will not give so-and-so a dirham at a time without giving him a dirham after it... and so on ad infinitum, then there is no doubt for a rational person about its permissibility, because its essence is the commitment of the one making the promise not to cut off the giving after beginning it. So if he is one who is not subject to breaking his promise, nor death for his essence, nor incapacity preventing the execution of his power and will, then we are certain that it will occur from him forever and we believe in it. And that is none other than God, our Master, exalted and glorious. This example is clearly applicable to what we claim regarding the bliss of Paradise for the believers, and also to what we claim regarding the punishment of Hellfire for the philosophers who assert the eternity of the world and their ilk among the naturalists and all the disbelievers. We ask Him, glorified, to make us in this world and the Hereafter among His successful party, upon whom no fear shall come, nor shall they grieve. Amen, O Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides an example to demonstrate the possibility of an infinite future series, which Sanusi uses to justify eternal bliss and punishment. He imagines a person who promises to give a dirham every successive time endlessly. This is logically possible because it is simply a commitment not to stop after starting. If the promiser is truthful, eternal, immune to death, and all-powerful, then such an infinite future series is certain. Since only God has these attributes, the promise of eternal reward and punishment is rationally conceivable and must be believed. Sanusi then applies this to Paradise for believers and Hell for philosophers and disbelievers who deny the world's creation. He ends with a prayer.

The Meaning of Fitnah as Trial by Hardship
Sanusi explains the term 'fitnah' in Surah Al-Ankabut quoting Zamakhshari and Ibn Atiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'fitnah' in Surah Al-Ankabut refers to a trial by severe obligations that tests the sincerity of faith.

Core Idea
Fitnah is a divine test, not just temptation.

This lesson explores a key term from Surah Al-Ankabut. Sanusi, quoting two major exegetes, clarifies that 'fitnah' here means a trial by hardships meant to distinguish sincere believers from insincere ones. Understanding this refines our grasp of divine testing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of fitnah and the list of trials.
The passage is a quotation from Zamakhshari followed by Ibn Atiyyah.
2
Identify the definition of fitnah
Zamakhshari defines fitnah as 'the trial by severe obligations.' Note the specific hardships he lists.
These include leaving homelands, jihad, difficult acts of obedience, and enduring harm.
3
Understand the purpose of fitnah
The trial is to distinguish the sincere from the insincere, the firm from the faltering. Consider why this distinction matters.
Fitnah reveals the truthfulness of one's faith through action.
4
Reflect on Ibn Atiyyah's comment
Ibn Atiyyah adds that truthfulness means actions confirm words. How does fitnah test this?
This connects the trial to the concept of verifying one's claim of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الفتنة عند الزمخشري عند تفسير قولهِ عزَّ وجلَّ : ( أَحَسِبَ النَّاسُ أَن يُتْرَكُوا أَن يَقُولُوا آمَنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفْتَنُونَ ] والفتنة : قال الزمخشري : ( هي الامتحان بشدائد التكليف ؛ مِنْ مفارقة الأوطان ، ومجاهدة الأعداء ، وسائر الطاعاتِ الشاقة ، وهجرِ الشهوات والملاذ ، وبالفقر والقحط ، وأنواع المصائب في الأنفسِ والأموال ، ومصابرةِ الكفَّارِ على أذاهم وكيدهم وضررهم . والمعنى : أَحسِبَ الذين أجروا كلمة الشهادة على ألسنتهم وأظهروا القول بالإيمانِ .. أنَّهمُ يُتركونَ كذلك غير ممتحنين ؟! بل يمتحنهم الله بضروب المحنِ حتى يبلو صبرهم ، وثبات أقدامهم وصحة عقائدهم، ونصوع نيَّاتِهم ؛ ليتميز المخلِصُ مِنْ غيرِ المخلص ، والراسخُ في الدينِ مِنَ المضطرب ، والمتمكِّنُ مِنَ العابدِ على حَرْفٍ ) انتهى . قالَ ابنُ عطيَّةَ : ) والصدق والكذبُ على بابهما ؛ أي : مَنْ صَدَّقَ فعله قولَهُ ، ومَنْ كَذَّبَهُ ( انتهى .
Explanation of the meaning of fitnah according to al-Zamakhshari when interpreting His statement, the Mighty and Majestic: “Do the people think that they will be left to say, ‘We believe’ and they will not be tested?” [Al-Ankabut: 2]. And al‑fitnah: al‑Zamakhshari said: “It is the trial by severe obligations: leaving homelands, struggling against enemies, all difficult acts of obedience, abandoning desires and pleasures, and by poverty, drought, and types of calamities in lives and wealth, and enduring the harm, deceit, and injury of the disbelievers. The meaning: Do those who have uttered the word of testimony on their tongues and displayed the statement of faith think that they will be left like that, untested? Rather, Allah tests them with various kinds of trials in order to test their patience, steadfastness, soundness of their beliefs, and sincerity of their intentions; so that the sincere may be distinguished from the insincere, the firm in religion from the faltering, and the established from the worshiper on the edge.” End. Ibn ‛Aṭiyyah said: “Truthfulness and falsehood are as they are; i.e., whoever verifies his statement by his action, and whoever falsifies it.” End.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a quotation and explanation. Sanusi first introduces the topic: an explanation of fitnah according to al-Zamakhshari in his commentary on the verse from Surah Al-Ankabut. Al-Zamakhshari defines fitnah as a trial through severe obligations of the shari'a, listing examples such as leaving homelands, jihad, difficult worships, and enduring poverty, calamities, and harm from disbelievers. The purpose is to test the sincerity of faith, to distinguish the sincere from the insincere, and to manifest the reality of one's belief. Then Sanusi cites Ibn ‛Aṭiyyah, who clarifies that the truthfulness and falsehood mentioned in the context refer to the correspondence between one's actions and one's words: the truthful person's actions confirm his words, whereas the liar's actions contradict them. Thus the fitnah serves to manifest this reality.

Key Terms
الفتنة (fitnah)
الامتحان (trial)
المخلص (sincere)
المضطرب (faltering)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fitnah only means temptation or sin.
Correction: In this verse, fitnah means trial by hardships to test faith, as defined by al-Zamakhshari.

Checkpoint
According to al-Zamakhshari, what is the primary meaning of 'fitnah' in the verse from Surah Al-Ankabut?
A) Disbelief and polytheism.
B) Trial by severe obligations and hardships.
C) Wealth and prosperity.
D) Punishment in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Fitnah in this context is a divine trial through hardships that tests the genuineness of one's faith, distinguishing the sincere from the insincere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a severe hardship—like losing a loved one, financial crisis, or persecution—do you instinctively see it as a test from Allah to prove your sincerity, or do you complain and question His wisdom? What specific step can you take today to change your response next time?
?
According to Ibn Atiyyah, truthfulness is when your actions match your words. Think of a recent promise you made to Allah (like praying on time or giving up a sin). Did your actions back up your words? If not, what does that reveal about your sincerity, and how will you correct it starting now?
?
This lesson says fitnah distinguishes the believer firmly anchored from the one worshiping on the edge. Are you the type of Muslim who is steadfast only when things are easy, but crumbles when tested? What is one area of your life where you are 'on the edge,' and what concrete change will you make to become firmly rooted in faith?

The True Meaning of Fitna in the Quran
Zamakhshari explains fitna as trials of faith including hardships, jihad, and patience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fitna in the Quran refers to the trials and tests of faith, and how these trials distinguish sincere believers from insincere ones.

Core Idea
Fitna is not merely temptation; it is a divine test that reveals the truth of one's faith.

When we read about fitna in the Quran, we often think of sin or temptation. However, in Surah al-Ankabut, Allah asks if people think they will be left untested. Zamakhshari explains that this fitna is the very test of faith through hardships and obligations. Let's explore his commentary to uncover the deeper meaning of this important term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation carefully. Notice how Zamakhshari expands on the verse.
The passage includes a quote from Ibn Atiyya at the end.
2
Identify the definition and examples of fitna
Find where Zamakhshari defines fitna as 'imtihan' (test) and lists examples such as leaving homelands, jihad, difficult acts of worship, etc. List them mentally.
These examples show that fitna covers both physical and spiritual hardships.
3
Understand the purpose of the test
Read the part where Zamakhshari explains that Allah tests people to distinguish the sincere from the insincere, the firmly rooted from the wavering. This is the key purpose.
The test is not punishment but a means of differentiation.
4
Reflect on Ibn Atiyya's remark
Ibn Atiyya adds that truthfulness and falsehood remain as they are, meaning actions confirm or belie words. Consider how this ties into the concept of fitna as a test of deeds.

Source Text

بيان معنى الفتنة عند الزمخشري عند تفسير قولهِ عزَّ وجلَّ : ( أَحَسِبَ النَّاسُ أَن يُتْرَكُوا أَن يَقُولُوا آمَنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفْتَنُونَ ] والفتنة : قال الزمخشري : ( هي الامتحان بشدائد التكليف ؛ مِنْ مفارقة الأوطان ، ومجاهدة الأعداء ، وسائر الطاعاتِ الشاقة ، وهجرِ الشهوات والملاذ ، وبالفقر والقحط ، وأنواع المصائب في الأنفسِ والأموال ، ومصابرةِ الكفَّارِ على أذاهم وكيدهم وضررهم والمعنى : أَحسِبَ الذين أجروا كلمة الشهادة على ألسنتهم وأظهروا القول بالإيمانِ .. أنَّهمُ يُتركونَ كذلك غير ممتحنين ؟! بل يمتحنهم الله بضروب المحنِ حتى يبلو صبرهم ، وثبات أقدامهم وصحة عقائدهم، ونصوع نيَّاتِهم ؛ ليتميز المخلِصُ مِنْ غيرِ المخلص ، والراسخُ في الدينِ مِنَ المضطرب ، والمتمكِّنُ مِنَ العابدِ على حَرْفٍ ) انتهى (1) قالَ ابنُ عطيَّةَ : ) والصدق والكذبُ على بابهما ؛ أي : مَنْ صَدَّقَ فعله قولَهُ ، ومَنْ كَذَّبَهُ ( انتهى (۲)
Clarification of the meaning of fitna according to al-Zamakhshari when interpreting His saying, May He be exalted: 'Do the people think that they will be left to say, "We believe" and they will not be tried?' And fitna: al-Zamakhshari said: 'It is the test through severe obligations: leaving homelands, struggling against enemies, all difficult acts of obedience, abandoning desires and pleasures, poverty and drought, various calamities in lives and wealth, and patience against the harm, cunning, and injury of the disbelievers. And the meaning: Do those who uttered the word of testimony on their tongues and manifested the statement of faith think that they will be left like that, untested? Rather, Allah tests them with various trials until He tries their patience, steadfastness, soundness of beliefs, and sincerity of intentions; so that the sincere may be distinguished from the insincere, the firmly rooted in religion from the wavering, and the established from the one who worships on the edge.' End. Ibn Atiyya said: 'And truthfulness and falsehood remain as they are; that is, whoever's action affirms his statement, and whoever's action belies it.' End.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage provides a tafsir (Quranic exegesis) explanation of the term fitna in the verse from Surah al-Ankabut. Zamakhshari defines fitna as the test (imtihan) through severe obligations (shada'id al-taklif). He enumerates examples: leaving homelands, jihad against enemies, difficult acts of worship, abandoning desires, poverty, drought, various calamities, and patience against the harm of disbelievers. Then he explains the verse's meaning: those who merely say 'we believe' verbally are not left untested; rather, Allah tests them to distinguish the sincere from the insincere, the firm from the wavering, and the established from the one who worships on the edge (i.e., conditionally). Then Ibn Atiyya adds a brief comment: truthfulness and falsehood remain as they are, meaning that a person's actions either confirm or contradict their words. This emphasizes that deeds verify claims of faith.

Key Terms
فتنة
امتحان
تكليف
مخلص
راسخ في الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fitna only means temptation or sin, especially in the context of the Dajjal or tribulations of the end times.
Correction: In this verse, fitna specifically means a divine test through hardships and obligations that distinguishes true believers from false claimants, not simply temptation to sin.

Checkpoint
According to Zamakhshari, what is the purpose of the fitna (trial)?
A) To punish people
B) To distinguish the sincere from the insincere
C) To make people fail
D) To increase worldly wealth

Key Takeaway
Fitna in the Quran means a divine test through hardships and obligations that reveals the sincerity of one's faith by distinguishing true believers from mere claimants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you faced a trial that made you question your own sincerity? What did it reveal about your faith? Will you ask Allah to strengthen your patience and steadfastness before the next test comes?
?
You claim to believe, but how would you fare if you were tested with losing your homeland, your wealth, or your loved ones? Is your worship firmly rooted, or are you worshipping on the edge? Commit today to one act of obedience that is difficult for you, as a preparation for greater trials.
?
Ibn Atiyya said: 'Whoever's action affirms his statement, and whoever's action belies it.' Look at your life: where is the gap between what you say and what you do? Will you close that gap starting today, or will you let the fitna expose your insincerity?

The Sunni View on Reward and Punishment
Sanusi states the Sunni position that reward is mere grace and punishment is mere justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that reward is purely an act of divine grace and punishment is purely an act of divine justice, and how this differs from rational necessitarianism.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment depend on God’s grace and justice, not on rational necessity.

Sanusi presents the definitive Sunni position on divine reward and punishment, clarifying that neither is a rational consequence of human actions. Instead, both are based on God’s free will and justice as communicated through revelation. This passage establishes the boundary between Sunni and Mu'tazili thought on this crucial topic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key figures
Notice that Sanusi mentions al-Imam (al-Ash'ari), al-Ghazali, and al-Qadi (al-Baqillani). Understand that al-Baqillani's view is the standard Sunni position.
Al-Imam here refers to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the founder of the Ash'ari school.
2
Extract the two central claims about reward and punishment
The passage states that reward is mere grace (fadl) and punishment is mere justice ('adl). Understand that these are not earned or owed but freely given by God.
3
Grasp why they are not rationally necessary
Sanusi emphasizes that reward and punishment are attached to command and prohibition by God's information (ikhbar), not by rational necessity. This distinguishes Sunni theology from Mu'tazili rationalism.
The phrase 'lazim 'aqlan' means 'rationally necessary'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوعيد.. لتحقق الأمرُ ، وخالفَهُ الإمامُ والغزالي ، وما صار إليهِ القاضي هو الجاري على قواعدِ أهل السنَّةِ ; فإِنَّ الثوابَ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى عندَهم مُجرَّدُ فضْلٍ ، والعقابَ مُجرَّدُ عَدْلٍ ، وتعلقهما بالأمر والنهي بإخبارِ اللهِ تعالى ، لا أنَّهما لازمان لهما عقلاً
And [the matter of] the threat... for the matter is established. Al-Imam (al-Ash'ari) and al-Ghazali disagreed with him. What al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) adopted is the one that flows on the principles of the Ahl al-Sunnah. For indeed, reward from God Almighty, according to them, is mere grace, and punishment is mere justice. Their attachment to command and prohibition is by God's information, not that they are necessarily entailed by them rationally.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement about the nature of divine reward and punishment. It begins by noting that the matter of the threat (al-wa'id) is established. Then it mentions that al-Imam (meaning Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and al-Ghazali disagreed with someone (likely the Mu'tazila or a earlier position). The correct view, according to al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani), is that which conforms to Sunni principles: reward is a mere grace (fadl) from God, not obligatory; punishment is mere justice (adl), not a necessary consequence. Both are attached to command and prohibition through God's informing (ikhbar), not because they are rationally necessary (lazim 'aqlan). This guards against the Mu'tazili doctrine that reward and punishment are rationally obligatory.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
فضل
عدل
إخبار
لازم عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward and punishment are rationally necessitated by obedience and disobedience.
Correction: They depend solely on God's grace and justice, as conveyed through revelation, not on rational deduction.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the Sunni position on reward and punishment?
A) They are rationally necessary consequences of obedience and disobedience.
B) They are mere grace and justice based on God's information.
C) They are earned by human effort.
D) They are both acts of grace.

Key Takeaway
For Ahl al-Sunnah, reward is a pure gift of grace and punishment is an act of justice, both based on God's information, not rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel entitled to Jannah because of your good deeds? How does the reality that reward is 'mere grace' humble your heart when you stand before Allah in prayer?
?
When you commit a sin, do you fear punishment more as a consequence you deserve or as a trial? Since punishment is pure justice, does that awareness make you more earnest in seeking forgiveness and reform?
?
If reward and punishment are not automatically tied to your actions but rely entirely on Allah's informing, does your trust in His promises and warnings shape how you live today? Where do you need to take His word more seriously?

First Sami' Proof: The Quranic Verse and Refutation of al-Jubba'i
Sanusi presents the Quranic verse (75:22-23) as proof and refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse (75:22-23) serves as a transmitted proof for the beatific vision and why al-Jubba'i's interpretation is linguistically and contextually invalid.

Core Idea
The grammar of the Quran itself confirms that believers will see Allah on the Day of Judgment.

This lesson examines the first transmitted proof (sam'i) for the beatific vision. Sanusi cites a key Quranic verse and explains why its phrasing necessarily indicates physical sight. He then presents an alternative interpretation by al-Jubba'i and systematically refutes it, demonstrating that the verse cannot mean anything other than vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its translation
Start by reading Quran 75:22-23 in Arabic and English. Notice the construction 'nazar ila' (look at).
The verse is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the linguistic point
Sanusi explains that 'nazar' with the preposition 'ila' means physical vision. Note the additional evidence: the verb is attributed to 'faces', which are the seat of the eyes.
This is a key grammatical argument.
3
Examine al-Jubba'i's alternative reading
Al-Jubba'i interprets 'nazar' as 'waiting' and treats 'ila' as a noun meaning 'blessing'. Carefully read this alternative.
This interpretation changes the entire meaning of the verse.
4
Follow the refutation
Sanusi rejects al-Jubba'i's view by pointing out that if it meant 'waiting', the verse would not be restricted to faces on that Day, since believers are always waiting. This makes the temporal adverb meaningless.
The refutation is concise but powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا السمعُ : فمنه قوله تعالى : ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَيْهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة : ٢٢-٢٣] ، وذلك لأنَّ النظر إذا تعدَّى بحرفِ ) إلى ( كانَ ظاهراً في معنى الرؤية ، ويُؤكِّدُ أنَّ المعني بهذا النظر الرؤية : إسنادُ هذا النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محلُّ العين الباصرة . وحمل الجبَّائي النظر في الآية على معنى الانتظار ، وجعل ) إلى ( اسماً بمعنى النعمة ، مفردَ الآلاءِ مضافاً لما بعده ، لا حرف جر ، والمعنى عندَهُ : منتظرةٌ نِعَمَ ربَّها ، فـ ( إلى ) عنده مفعول بـ ( ناظرة . ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ لو أُريدَ ذلكَ لَمَا خُصَّ بإسناده إلى الوجوه ، ولم يكن للتقييد بالظرف ـ وهو ( يومئذٍ ) - معنى ؛ فإنَّ المؤمنينَ لم يزالوا في الدارِ الدنيا منتظرين نعمة الله تعالى وآلاءه سبحانه ، بل الكفَّارُ في الدنيا كذلك
As for the transmitted proof (sam'): among it is His statement, Exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (Q 75:22-23). And that is because 'nazar' (looking) when it is transitive with the particle 'ila' is manifest in the meaning of vision (ru'ya). And that what is meant by this 'nazar' is vision is confirmed by the attribution of this 'nazar' to the face which is the locus of the seeing eye. Al-Jubba'i interpreted 'nazar' in the verse to mean waiting (intizar), and made 'ila' a noun meaning 'blessing' (ni'ma), singular of 'ala', annexed to what follows, not a preposition. So according to him the meaning is 'awaiting the blessings of their Lord'. And 'ila' for him is the object of 'nazira'. And it was refuted: that if that were intended, it would not have been specifically attributed to faces, and the restriction by the adverb 'on that day' would have no meaning; for the believers have never ceased in the abode of this world to be awaiting the blessing of Allah, Exalted, and His favors, and even the disbelievers in this world are likewise.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents the first transmitted (sam'i) proof from the Quran. Sanusi cites verse 75:22-23, where 'nazar' (looking) is transitive with the preposition 'ila', which in Arabic usage indicates physical sight (ru'ya). He strengthens this by noting that the act is attributed to 'faces', which are the seat of the eyes. Then he mentions al-Jubba'i's alternative reading: he took 'nazar' to mean 'waiting' and 'ila' as a noun meaning 'blessing'. The refutation follows: if it meant waiting, it would not be specifically attributed to faces on that Day, since believers (and even disbelievers) are constantly awaiting God's blessings in this world. The restriction 'on that day' would be pointless. Thus the correct meaning is vision.

Key Terms
سمع
نظر
رؤية
انتظار
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 75:22-23 can be interpreted to mean 'awaiting blessings' rather than 'seeing Allah'.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the linguistic structure (nazar ila + attribution to faces + restriction to that day) necessarily indicates physical vision, not waiting.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject al-Jubba'i's interpretation of the verse?
A) Because al-Jubba'i was a known liar.
B) Because if it meant waiting, it would not be restricted to faces on that Day, and believers already wait in this world.
C) Because the verse uses the word 'wujuh' (faces) which cannot see.
D) Because al-Jubba'i's grammar is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
Quran 75:22-23 linguistically and contextually proves that believers will see Allah on the Day of Judgment, and al-Jubba'i's interpretation fails because it ignores the specific wording and temporal restriction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly anticipate the vision of your Lord with a longing that surpasses all worldly desires, or has the hope of seeing Him become a distant platitude? What one change in your daily life would prove that you are 'radiant' in awaiting that moment?
?
Al-Jubba'i reduced the verse to a mundane waiting for blessings. Have you ever dulled the Quran's sharp promises by interpreting them in a way that costs you nothing? Examine one verse you've been avoiding taking literally—what would it demand of you if you accepted its apparent meaning?
?
If the refutation of al-Jubba'i hinges on the specific words of the Quran, how carefully do you handle Allah's speech? Are you willing to submit to what the text plainly says, even when it challenges your comfort or intellect? Commit to reading the Quran this week with a heart that asks, 'What do these words require of me?'

Third Sami' Proof: Consensus and Hadith on Ru'ya
Sanusi cites consensus of the salaf and a prophetic hadith to prove ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the salaf and a prophetic hadith establish the reality of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The salaf's consensus and a clear hadith prove believers will see Allah without obstruction.

Sanusi presents the third transmitted proof for ru'ya: the consensus of the righteous predecessors and a well-known hadith. This passage shows that the early Muslims prayed for this vision and the Prophet himself guaranteed it, using a vivid simile to emphasize clarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Note the two pieces of evidence cited.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا تضامون' and its explanation.
2
Understand the role of consensus
Recognize that ijma' of the salaf is a valid proof in kalam. Their collective desire for ru'ya indicates it is possible and real.
3
Analyze the hadith simile
The moon analogy is about clarity and absence of crowding, not about Allah's nature. Discuss why this distinction matters.
This guards against the misconception of tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ : إجماعُ السلف الصالح على الرغبة إلى الله تعالى أنْ يُمتعهم بالنظر إلى وجهه الكريم ، وقد ورد ذلكَ في بعض أدعية النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، ومنها حديث : ( سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ كَمَا تَرَوْنَ الْقَمَرَ لَيْلَةَ الْبَدْرِ ، لاَ تُضَامُونَ - أو : لا تُضَارُّونَ - فِي الرُّؤْيَةِ ) ، ووجه التشبيه بالقمرِ : ما أشار إليهِ آخرُ الحديثِ مِنْ عدمِ تضار بعضهم ببعض
And among the proofs: the consensus (ijma') of the righteous predecessors (salaf) on desiring from Allah, Exalted, that He grant them the vision of His noble face. And that has been reported in some supplications of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Among them is the hadith: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon, you will not be overcrowded — or: you will not be harmed — in seeing Him.' And the reason for the similitude with the moon is what the end of the hadith indicates: the lack of crowding of some by others.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents the third transmitted proof: the consensus (ijma') of the early righteous Muslims (salaf) who used to pray to be granted the vision of Allah's face. This prayer is also found in some supplications of the Prophet. Then Sanusi cites the well-known hadith that believers will see their Lord like they see the full moon without crowding. The analogy to the moon is not about shape or visibility conditions, but rather about the clarity and the fact that each beholder will have a clear view without obstruction, as the end of the hadith explains.

Key Terms

سلف
حديث
رؤية
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simile to the moon suggests Allah is like the moon in appearance.
Correction: The analogy is about visibility and clarity; Allah is not like any created thing.

Checkpoint

A) That Allah is similar to the moon in appearance.
B) That the vision will be clear and without crowding.
C) That only one person can see at a time.
D) That the vision will be at night.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the salaf and the prophetic hadith confirm that believers will see Allah in the afterlife, clearly and without obstruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refutation of the interpretation of 'nāẓirah' as expecting
Sanusi refutes Zamakhshari's interpretation of 'nāẓirah' as expecting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the interpretation of 'nāẓirah' as 'expecting' based on grammatical and contextual evidence.

Core Idea
A word's meaning in the Quran is determined by its grammatical context, not just lexical possibilities.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to a specific Quranic interpretation by the famous scholar Zamakhshari. The debate is about the word 'nāẓirah' in the verse about the faces looking at their Lord on the Day of Judgment. Using careful linguistic reasoning, Sanusi shows why this word must mean 'seeing' and not 'expecting'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the compact style of refutation.
The passage begins with 'It was refuted', indicating a response to a prior claim.
2
Identify the two arguments
Sanusi gives two reasons: first, if it meant 'expecting', it would not be specifically attributed to 'faces'; second, the time restriction 'on that day' would be meaningless. Find these in the text.
The arguments are connected by 'and' and a semicolon.
3
Reflect on the method
Notice how Sanusi uses the grammatical structure (attribution to faces) and contextual clue (time phrase) to refute the interpretation. This shows the importance of linguistic context in Quranic exegesis.
This method is common in kalam and tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ لو أُريدَ ذلكَ لَمَا خُصَّ بإسناده إلى الوجوه ، ولم يكن للتقييد بالظرف ـ وهو ( يومئذٍ ) - معنى ؛ فإنَّ المؤمنينَ لم يزالوا في الدارِ الدنيا منتظرين نعمة الله تعالى وآلاءه سبحانه ، بل الكفَّارُ في الدنيا كذلك.
It was refuted: if that were intended, it would not have been specifically attributed to faces, and the restriction to the time phrase — which is 'on that day' — would have no meaning; for the believers have always in the abode of this world been expecting the blessing of God Almighty and His favors, and even the disbelievers in this world are likewise.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Zamakhshari's claim that the word 'nāẓirah' in the verse (75:23) means 'expecting' rather than 'seeing'. The refutation proceeds in two steps. First, if 'expecting' were meant, why would the verb be specifically attributed to 'faces'? Expectation is not peculiar to faces. Second, the restriction to 'on that day' would be meaningless because believers are always expecting God's blessings in this world, and even disbelievers expect things. Thus the context forces the meaning of 'seeing'.

Key Terms
ناظرة
منتظرة
الوجوه
يومئذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nāẓirah' can mean 'expecting' in any context.
Correction: The context of the verse (attribution to faces and restriction to the Day of Judgment) forces the meaning of 'seeing', not 'expecting'.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects interpreting 'nāẓirah' as 'expecting'?
A) Because expectation is impossible for God.
B) Because the attribute 'faces' is not specific to expectation, and the time phrase would be meaningless.
C) Because the verse is a metaphor.
D) Because believers never expect anything.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of a Quranic word must be determined by its grammatical and contextual cues, not merely by lexical options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse, do you pay attention to its grammatical structure and context, or do you rely on your own assumptions about what the words mean? What is one verse you might have misunderstood due to ignoring context?
?
Have you ever interpreted a Quranic verse in a way that suited your desire, ignoring the linguistic clues that point to its true meaning? How will you commit to studying the Quran with more care from now on?
?
The refutation shows that even a single word's meaning can be constrained by context. In what areas of your life do you ignore context and assume meanings that suit you—e.g., in your relationships, your duties, or your understanding of yourself? How can you apply the principle of contextual integrity to align with truth?
