Proofs for the Vision of God: Consensus and Hadith
Sanusi cites consensus and a prophetic hadith as evidence for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the righteous predecessors and the hadith of seeing God like the full moon serve as evidence for the vision of God, and why the comparison does not imply God has a body or direction.

Core Idea
Seeing God is like seeing the full moon—clear and unobstructed, not physical.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his argument for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife. He cites two authoritative sources: the unanimous practice of the early Muslims (salaf) who supplicated for this vision, and a famous prophetic hadith that compares the vision to seeing the full moon. The key is to understand what the comparison means—and what it does not mean.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the two pieces of evidence: the consensus of the salaf and the hadith.
2
Identify the two proofs
First, find where Sanusi mentions the consensus of the salaf praying to see God. Then, locate the hadith text. Understand that both are used as proofs for the vision being possible and desirable.
3
Understand the clarification about the moon analogy
Sanusi explicitly says the comparison to the moon is not about God being like the moon. Rather, it is about the vision itself: clear, unobstructed, and without crowding. Note the terms 'direction' (al-jiha) and 'corporeality' (al-jismiyya) are denied.
This clarification guards against misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Look at the Arabic key terms: إجماع السلف, حديث الرؤية, تشبيه بالقمر, الجهة, الجسمية. Try to connect each to its role in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ : إجماعُ السلف الصالح على الرغبة إلى الله تعالى أنْ يُمتعهم بالنظر إلى ه الكريم ، وقد ورد ذلكَ في بعض أدعية النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، ومنها حديث : ( سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ كَمَا تَرَوْنَ الْقَمَرَ لَيْلَةَ الْبَدْرِ ، لاَ تُضَامُونَ - أو : لا تُضَارُّونَ - فِي الرُّؤْيَةِ ) ، ووجه التشبيه بالقمرِ : ما أشار إليهِ آخرُ الحديثِ مِنْ عدمِ تضار بعضهم ببعض وقت الرؤية ، أمَّا الجهة والجسمية ولوازمهما فمستحيلةٌ في حقه تعالى وبالجملة : فالمقصود تشبيه الرؤية بالرؤيةِ فيما ذُكِرَ ، لا المرئي بالمرئي.
And among the proofs is the consensus of the righteous predecessors (salaf) on supplicating to God Almighty to let them enjoy looking at His noble face, and that has been transmitted in some of the Prophet's supplications, including the hadith: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon, you will not be harmed — or: you will not be harmed — in seeing.' And the point of the comparison to the moon is what the end of the hadith indicates about the absence of crowding each other at the time of vision. As for direction, corporeality, and their implications, they are impossible with respect to Him. In summary, the intention is to liken the vision to vision in what was mentioned, not the visible to the visible.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage provides two more proofs for the vision of God. First, the consensus of the righteous predecessors (the salaf) who prayed to God to enjoy looking at His noble face. This consensus indicates that vision is possible and desirable. Second, the well-known hadith in which the Prophet says believers will see their Lord like they see the moon on a full moon night, with no crowding. The author clarifies the point of the comparison: it is about the clarity and lack of obstruction in the vision itself, not about the object being like the moon (i.e., God is not a physical body). Direction and corporeality remain impossible for God. Thus the vision is real but not physical.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the comparison means God is like the moon or has a physical body.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the comparison is about the clarity of the vision, not the nature of God. Direction and corporeality are impossible for Him.

Checkpoint
What does the hadith compare the vision of God to?
A) Seeing the sun.
B) Seeing the moon on a full moon night.
C) Seeing a bright star.
D) Seeing the reflection in water.

Key Takeaway
The vision of God is affirmed through consensus and hadith, but it is a vision of clarity, not a physical sight of a material being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah as the salaf did, with a yearning that shapes your daily choices? Or do you live as if this vision is irrelevant to your present life?
?
The hadith promises a vision as clear as the full moon. Does your certainty about meeting Allah affect how you deal with doubt and distractions today? What one thing would change if you were absolutely sure you will see Him tomorrow?
?
How often do you pray for the joy of looking at Allah's face? If it is rare, what does that say about your attachment to this world versus the afterlife? Commit now to say this prayer sincerely tonight.

The Eighth Objection and the Tilmisani Response
Sanusi records the eighth objection and the Tilmisani response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth objection to the proof for ru'ya and the initial response provided by a Tilmisani scholar.

Core Idea
The opponent challenges the limited enumeration of shared attributes, but a Tilmisani scholar defends using 'I investigated and did not find'.

In this lesson, we encounter the eighth objection to the rational proof for seeing God. The opponent concedes that existence is shared but denies that it is limited to existence and temporality, proposing possibility as another shared attribute. We then see a Tilmisani scholar's response, arguing that the proponent's investigation suffices and that the opponent must invalidate any new attribute proposed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the opponent's main points: concession of shared existence, denial of limited enumeration, proposal of possibility or composite, and the argument against relying on 'not finding'.
The objection is strong, as noted by Sanusi.
2
Examine the Tilmisani response
Understand the Tilmisani's defense: the proponent's investigation suffices, and the burden falls on the opponent to invalidate any new description. Also note his attempt to invalidate possibility alone.
The Tilmisani's invalidation uses the impossibility of seeing every possible thing.
3
Reflect on the strengths and weaknesses
Consider the initial plausibility of the response. Does the 'I investigated and did not find' claim provide certainty? Sanusi will later critique this.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنُ : سلَّمنا أنَّ مفهوم الوجودِ مشترك ، لكن لا تُسلَّم أن لا مشترك سوى الوجودِ والحدوثِ ، وحصركم منخرم بالإمكان أو بالمركب منه ومِنْ غيرِه
وهذا منع قوي ، والاعتماد على عدم الوجدانِ لا يفيد العلم ، ولا يمكن إبطال التعليل بالإمكان أو بالمركَبِ منهُ ومِنْ غيرِهِ بأَنَّ الإمكان أمر عدمي ؛ فإنَّ الخصم يقولُ ذلك في صحة الرؤية ، ولا يمتنع تعليلُ العدمي بالعدمي .
قلتُ : أَجابَ عنهُ بعضُ التِّلِمْسانيين في شرحه على عقيدة ابن الحاجب) » بأنْ قالَ(۲) : ( يكفي المستدل : « بحثتُ فلم أجد » ، ثم ظهور وصفِ صالح للتعليل بعد إبطالِ ما حُصِرَ مِنَ الأوصافِ (۳) .. لا يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، فيتعيَّنُ إبطاله ) (٤) ، ثم أبطل عليَّةَ الإمكان منفرداً : بعدمِ صحة رؤية كلٌّ ممكن(٥) ، ومعَ
(7)
(۱) القاضي والإمام يقولان : الوجود نفس الموجود ، لكنه ليس عين ماهيته وتمامها كما يقول الأشعري ، فالاشتراك عندهما معنوي
(۲) هو العلامة ابن زكري كما تقدم أكثر من مرة .
(۳) في الأصل المنقول عنه زيادة : ( بالبحث ) .
(٤) انظر ( بغية الطالب في شرح عقيدة ابن الحاجب » ( ص ٢٥٧ ) .
(٥) إذ من الممكنات ما زال في طي العدم، والمعدوم لا يُرى ، فالإطلاق غير صحيح ؛ إذ لم يقل بصحة رؤية المعدوم إلا السالمية
(٦) كالإمكان مع الحدوث أو بشرطه
باستحالة التركيب في العلَّةِ العقليّةِ
قلتُ : ولا يخفى ضَعفُهُ ؛ فإنَّ قولَ المستدل : ( بحثتُ فلم أجد ) إنَّما يُحصِّلُ الظَّنَّ فقط ، فيصحُ قَبولُهُ في الأماراتِ وما المطلوب منه الظن ، لا في البراهين وما المطلوب منه العلم ؛ كمسألتنا هذهِ وإنَّما يصح الاستدلال بالسبرِ في مثلِ مسألتنا إذا كان الحصر قطعياً ؛ لدورانِهِ بينَ النفْي والإثباتِ ، والإبطال قطعياً ؛ لكونِهِ مِنَ الضرورياتِ أو ما ينتهي إليها ، وأين ذلك ، وما ذكر بعد مبني على هذا الأساس الذي بان انهدامه ؟!(۱) على أنَّ إبطالَهُ عليَّةَ الإمكان منفرداً بعدم صحةِ رؤية كلُّ ممكن .. فاسد ؛ لأنَّا نقول : الممتنع وقوع رؤية كل ممكن ، لا صحته (۲) ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ صحةِ الشيء وقوعه ، والمعلَّلُ بالإمكان الثاني لا الأوَّلِ ، والله تعالى أعلم
The eighth [objection]: We concede that the concept of existence is shared, but it is not conceded that there is no shared [attribute] except existence and temporality, and your enumeration is breached by possibility or by the composite of it and something else. This is a strong objection, and reliance on not finding [another shared attribute] does not yield knowledge, and it is not possible to invalidate the causal explanation by possibility or by the composite of it and something else by saying that possibility is a non-existential matter; for the opponent says that concerning the validity of vision, and there is no impossibility in explaining the non-existential by the non-existential. I say: One of the Tilmisani scholars responded to him in his commentary on the Creed of Ibn al-Hajib, saying: “It suffices for the proponent: ‘I investigated and did not find [any other shared attribute]’, then the appearance of a description suitable for causal explanation after invalidating what was enumerated from the descriptions does not necessitate his being silenced; rather it is necessary to invalidate it [the alternative description].” Then he invalidated the causality of possibility alone by the unsoundness of seeing every possible thing, and with [the impossibility of composition in the intellectual cause]. I say: Its weakness is not hidden; for the proponent’s saying “I investigated and did not find” only yields conjecture, so it is acceptable in indications where conjecture is required, not in proofs where knowledge is required, as in our question. The method of scanning (sabr) is only valid in a case like ours if the enumeration is certain, due to its revolving between negation and affirmation, and the invalidation is certain, because it is from among necessary [knowledge] or what leads to it. Where is that? And what was mentioned afterwards is built on this foundation whose collapse is evident? Moreover, his invalidation of the causality of possibility alone by the unsoundness of seeing every possible thing is corrupt; for we say: The occurrence of seeing every possible thing is impossible, not its soundness, and from the soundness of a thing its occurrence does not follow, and the thing explained is by the second possibility, not the first. And God Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection to the rational proof for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya), followed by a response from a Tilmisani scholar and the author's critique. The objection concedes that the concept of existence is shared between God and creatures, but denies that the only shared attributes are existence and temporality. The opponent argues that the proponent's enumeration is breached by possibility (imkan) or by a composite of possibility and something else. The opponent also argues that relying on not finding another shared attribute does not yield certain knowledge, and that one cannot invalidate a causal explanation by possibility by claiming possibility is non-existential, since the opponent can say the same about vision. The author then reports a Tilmisani response: it suffices for the proponent to say 'I investigated and did not find,' and if a new description appears after invalidating the enumerated ones, the proponent is not obligated to address it; rather the opponent must invalidate that new description. The Tilmisani then attempted to invalidate possibility alone by noting that not every possible thing can be seen. The author criticizes this response: the 'investigated and did not find' claim only gives conjecture, not certainty, while the proof for ru'ya requires certain knowledge. The method of scanning (sabr) requires certain enumeration and certain invalidation, which is not present here. Furthermore, the invalidation of possibility alone fails because the impossibility is of the occurrence of seeing every possible thing, not of its soundness; soundness does not entail occurrence. The causal explanation uses the second type of possibility (the absolute possibility of existence), not the first (the possibility of happening). Thus the objection remains unanswered.

Key Terms
وجود
حدوث
إمكان
حصر
سبر
رؤية

Checkpoint
What does the opponent in the eighth objection propose as a shared attribute that breaches the enumeration of existence and temporality?
A) Only existence
B) Temporality alone
C) Possibility or a composite of possibility and something else
D) Non-existential attributes

Key Takeaway
The eighth objection challenges the limited enumeration of shared attributes, but the Tilmisani scholar defends the proponent by arguing that 'I investigated' suffices and attempts to invalidate possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological objection that you cannot immediately answer, do you, like the Tilmisani, hold firm to what you have verified, or do you let doubt erode your certainty? What is one area of your faith where you need to say 'I investigated and did not find' and trust that investigation?
?
This lesson shows that arguments about God's attributes require rigorous intellectual work. Are you willing to engage in that work, or do you prefer to avoid difficult questions? What step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of one of God's attributes?
?
The opponent argues that not finding a shared attribute does not produce knowledge. How does this challenge you to examine the basis of your own beliefs? Are you relying on personal investigation or on the authority of scholars? What will you do to strengthen your conviction?

Sanusi's Critique of the Tilmisani Response
Sanusi critiques the Tilmisani response and defends the original proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's critique of the Tilmisani response and why the proof for ru'ya remains unrefuted.

Core Idea
The 'I investigated' claim only yields conjecture, not the certainty required for demonstrative proofs.

In this lesson, we examine Sanusi's own evaluation of the Tilmisani response. He argues that the method of 'scanning' (sabr) requires certain enumeration and invalidation, and that the Tilmisani's invalidation of possibility is flawed because it confuses soundness with occurrence. This critique reinforces the need for rigorous proof in matters of faith.

Guided Path
1
Analyze Sanusi's criticism of the 'investigate' approach
Sanusi states that 'I investigated and did not find' yields only conjecture (zann), not certainty ('ilm). Understand why this matters for the proof for ru'ya.
Conjecture is acceptable in some legal matters but not in doctrinal proofs.
2
Understand the conditions for valid sabr (scanning)
Sanusi explains that sabr requires certain enumeration and certain invalidation, which is not present here. Consider what would make enumeration certain.
Sanusi implies that the opponent's objection has not been fully answered.
3
Evaluate the flaw in the invalidation of possibility
Sanusi points out that the Tilmisani argued that not every possible thing can be seen, but that proves impossibility of occurrence, not unsoundness of possibility as an attribute. Also note the distinction between first and second possibility.
This shows the subtlety of the argument.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the proponent's claim 'I investigated and did not find' insufficient for the proof of ru'ya?
A) Because the opponent may have found more shared attributes
B) Because it yields only conjecture, not certainty
C) Because the proof does not require investigation
D) Because the claim is false

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on a certain belief about Allah—like that He will be seen in the afterlife—but have you ever settled for mere conjecture (ظن) instead of seeking certain knowledge (علم)? Where in your faith are you content with 'I think so' rather than 'I know so'? What will you do today to move from conjecture to certainty?
?
When you face a doubt about your creed, do you quickly accept a response because it sounds plausible, without examining whether it meets the standard of certainty? Recall a time you did that. Will you now go back and test that response by the criteria Sanusi lays out?
?
Sanusi shows that confusing 'soundness' with 'occurrence' leads to error. In your own life, have you ever assumed that because something did not happen, it could not happen (or was not sound)? How might this confusion be weakening your trust in Allah's power or promises? Commit to one way you will correct this misstep this week.

The Ninth Objection: Temporality as a Shared Attribute
Sanusi records the start of the ninth objection questioning temporality's exclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the ninth objection in Sanusi's argument about shared attributes between God and creation.

Core Idea
The ninth objection concedes shared attributes but disputes temporality's exclusion.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the beginning of the ninth objection against the claim that only existence is a valid shared attribute between God and creatures. The objector concedes that temporality and existence are the only candidates, but argues that temporality should not be dismissed. The text then quotes a statement that temporality implies non-existence, and begins a rebuttal that is cut off on this page.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the tone and the structure of the objection.
The passage is incomplete; the full response continues on the next page.
2
Identify the concession
The objector starts by conceding that there is no shared attribute except temporality and existence. This sets the scope of the dispute.
3
Locate the point of dispute
The objector then says they do not concede that temporality falls from consideration. This is the core of the objection.
The dispute is about whether temporality can be a shared attribute despite being linked to non-existence.
4
Analyze the quoted statement
The objector quotes the proponent's claim: 'It is not intelligible except with a share of non-existence.' This is the basis for excluding temporality.
Consider why the proponent might say this about temporality.
5
Observe the beginning of the rebuttal
The objector begins to reject the quote with 'We say: it is not conceded, rather...' but the text ends. Think about what arguments might follow.
This incomplete part hints at a deeper theological exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : سلَّمنا أن لا مشترك سوى الحدوث والوجودِ ، لكن لا نُسلّم سقوط الحدوثِ عن درجة الاعتبار

Commentary
This passage begins the ninth objection, which concedes that the only shared attributes between God and creatures are temporality and existence, but disputes the claim that temporality is excluded from consideration as a shared attribute. The objector quotes a statement (likely from the proponent) that temporality 'is not intelligible except with a share of non-existence' (i.e., it inherently implies prior non-existence, so it cannot be an attribute of God). The objector denies this and begins a rebuttal with 'We say: it is not conceded, rather…', but the text cuts off at the end of the page. The argument is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms

A) That temporality and existence are the only shared attributes.

?

Where in your own faith have you stopped questioning because the answer seemed comfortable? How will you actively challenge your assumptions about God's attributes this week?
?
The objector quotes the proponent's reason—that temporality requires non-existence—and begins to reject it. Are there aspects of God you have denied based on your own limited understanding of what is 'beneath' Him? Can you identify one quality you have silently excluded from God because you thought it implied imperfection? What would change in your worship if you reconsidered that exclusion?

Refutation of the Ray Theory of Vision
Sanusi refutes the ray theory of vision using logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the ray theory of vision using logical reasoning and empirical observation.

Core Idea
Vision by emission cannot explain seeing larger objects or seeing in darkness.

Sanusi presents a clear logical refutation of the ray theory of vision, a theory held by some philosophers. He shows that if vision were by rays emitted from the eye, we could only see objects as large as our pupil. Then he addresses a counterargument and provides a final decisive point. This lesson walks through the steps of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a logical conditional: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the main logical consequence
Find the consequence Sanusi derives from the ray theory: that a person would see only the size of their pupil. Reflect on why this follows.
3
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponents claim that the ray connects with luminous air to see larger objects. See how they analogize to crystal.
This is a common counterargument from those who hold the ray theory.
4
Examine the two-part refutation
Sanusi replies: first, even with air, one would see only the pupil's size of air; second, we see even when air is dark. Understand both points.
The second point is particularly strong because it uses an empirical observation.
5
Summarize the argument
Write a short summary of the entire refutation in your own words. This will help solidify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وذلك لو صح لوجب ... ( إلى آخرِهِ : هذا مِنْ جملة ما رُدَّ بهِ عليهمُ القولُ بانبعاث الأشعة ؛ وهو أنَّه لو كانَتِ الرؤية بانبعاث الأشعة لزمَ ألا يرى الإنسانُ إلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ؛ إِذْ لا تسع حدقتُهُ مِنَ الأشعة أكثر منها ، لكنَّهُ يرى دفعةً أكثرَ مِنْ ذاتِهِ كلَّها بأضعافٍ عن حدقتِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ الرؤية ليسَتْ بما يزعمونَ مِن انبعاث الأشعة . قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لاتصال الشعاع بالهواء وهو مضيء ، فأعانَ على رؤية ما قابله ، كالبلورِ المُعِينِ بإشراقه على رؤية ما فيه . قلنا : فيلزمُ أَلا يَرى مِنَ الهواء إلا قدر حدقتِهِ ، وأيضاً : فنحن نرى والهواء مظلم ما نراه والهواء مشرق .
His statement: '(And that, if true, would necessitate...' up to the end: This is part of what was used to refute them regarding the claim of emission of rays. It is that if vision were by emission of rays, it would necessarily follow that a person sees only the size of his pupil, since his pupil cannot contain more rays than itself; but he sees at once more than all of his own being by multiples of his pupil. So this indicates that vision is not, as they claim, based on emission of rays. They said: That is because the ray connects with the air, which is luminous, so it helps in seeing what is opposite it, like crystal which aids by its illumination in seeing what is inside it. We said: Then it would follow that one sees from the air only the size of his pupil; and also: we see, with the air dark, what we see, and with the air bright?
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ray theory of vision. It begins by summarizing the argument: if vision were by emission of rays from the eye, then a person could only see an object the size of his own pupil, because only that amount of rays can emanate from the pupil. However, we see objects much larger than the pupil, which contradicts the theory. The opponents respond by claiming that the ray connects with the surrounding air, which is luminous, and this helps to see larger objects, similar to how a crystal can illuminate its contents. The refutation replies with two points: first, even if the air helps, the ray itself is only from the pupil, so the air would only be seen in proportion to the pupil's size; second, we are able to see even when the air is dark, which shows that the ray theory fails because the ray would have no light to propagate. Thus, the ray theory is logically inconsistent and cannot explain our actual experience of vision.

Key Terms
انبعاث الأشعة (inbi‘āth al-ash‘a‘a)
الحدقة (al-ḥadaqa)
الهواء (al-hawā‘)
البلور (al-ballūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ray theory can be saved by appealing to a luminous medium such as air.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even with a luminous medium, the size limitation persists (only the pupil's worth of air is illuminated) and furthermore we see in darkness, which the theory cannot explain.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Sanusi argue follows from the ray theory of vision?
A) We would see nothing in the dark.
B) We would only see objects the size of our pupil.
C) We would see everything upside down.
D) We would see only our own eye.

Key Takeaway
The ray theory of vision is logically inconsistent because it cannot account for seeing objects larger than the pupil or seeing in darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the theories you hold about the unseen—such as how Allah's attributes relate to His creation—are you as willing to subject them to logical scrutiny as Sanusi subjects the ray theory? What would it take for you to abandon a cherished belief if reason and revelation both contradicted it?
?
The opponents in this passage clung to their theory by adding an ad hoc explanation (the luminous air). In your own life, where have you added weak excuses to avoid accepting a difficult truth? Will you commit to removing those excuses today?
?
If your vision—something you rely on daily—can work in ways that defy a plausible theory, how much more should you be humble about your ability to grasp the unseen? What assumption about Allah or the afterlife are you currently holding that may need re-examination in light of sound kalam?

Miracles vs. Magic: The Role of Ordinary Causes
Sanusi defines the difference between miracle and magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between miracles (mu'jizat) and magic (sihr) according to Sanusi, focusing on the presence of an ordinary cause as the distinguishing factor.

Core Idea
Miracles are uncaused by natural means; magic has an ordinary cause.

In this passage, Sanusi begins by clarifying how miracles are distinct from magic. He explains that while both may appear extraordinary, magic always operates through a consistent, ordinary cause, whereas a miracle does not. This foundational distinction helps prevent confusion about the nature of prophetic miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the definitions and the scholarly disagreement mentioned.
Notice that Sanusi starts with a clear contrast: magic has a regular cause, miracles do not.
2
Identify Sanusi's main criterion
Find the sentence that explicitly states what distinguishes a miracle from magic. Look for the phrase contrasting 'ordinary cause' with the lack thereof.
The key is in the first few words: 'magic has an ordinary cause linked to it, unlike the miracle.'
3
Note the scholarly disagreement
Ibn 'Arafah defines magic as extraordinary but tied to a cause. Al-Qarafi disagrees, claiming it is not truly extraordinary. Understand that both views still affirm magic has a cause, unlike miracles.
This shows that even among scholars, the nature of magic is debated, but the cause-based distinction from miracles remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرف الفرق بين المعجزة والسحر؛ وهو أن السحر له سبب عادي يرتبط به، بخلاف المعجزة. ولهذا عرَّف الشيخ ابن عرفة السحر بقوله: أمر خارق للعادة، مطرد الارتباط به. قال: وزعم القرافي أنه غير خارق للعادة، وغرابته إنما هو بسبب خاص للمبالغة في البعد. قال العلامة العكاري في حاشيته: حتى يتوهم فيه مخالطة السحرة ونحوهم. وبقوله: مقارن لدعوى الرسالة مما وقع بدون دعوى، أو بدعوى غير دعوى الرسالة؛ كدعوى الولاية. هذا الذي ذكرت مما تتميز به المعجزة عن الكرامة؛ وذلك أن الكرامة وإن كانت أمرا خارقا للعادة فإنها لا تكون مقارنة لدعوى النبوة، وبهذا يزول اللبس بينهما. ومن أئمتنا من ذهب إلى أن الفرق بينهما: أن الكرامة لا تقع على اختيار وقصد من الولي بخلاف المعجزة، والمراد بالاختيار والإرادة هنا: الشهوة والتمني؛ إذ الفعل الخارق قد يكون من غير جنس مقدور العبد ومراده. ومن الأئمة من فرق بينهما: بأن كل ما وقع من الخوارق معجزة لنبي لا يقع كرامة لولي؛ كإحياء الموتى، وإبراء الأكمه، وقلب العصا حية، وفلق البحر أطوادا. والأستاذ يصرح بمنع هذا ومنع غيره من الخوارق على يد الأولياء، وإنما يجوز ما يجري مجرى إجابة الدعوة، ووجود ماء في البرية، وغير ذلك مما يكرم الله به عباده ولا يبلغ خوارق العادات.
And by this you know the difference between the miracle (al-mu'jizah) and magic (al-sihr): that magic has an ordinary cause linked to it, unlike the miracle. For this reason, Shaykh Ibn 'Arafah defined magic as: an extraordinary matter, consistently linked to it. He said: And al-Qarafi claimed that it is not extraordinary, and its strangeness is only due to a special cause for exaggeration in remoteness. Al-'Akkari said in his gloss: so that one may imagine the mixing of sorcerers and the like. And by his saying: (accompanying the claim of messengership) [the miracle is distinguished from] what occurs without a claim, or with a claim other than the claim of messengership, such as the claim of sainthood. This which I have mentioned is what distinguishes the miracle from the saintly wonder (al-karamah); that is, even if the saintly wonder is an extraordinary matter, it is not accompanied by the claim of prophethood, and by this the confusion between them is removed. Among our imams are those who held that the difference between them is: that the saintly wonder does not occur by choice and intent of the saint, unlike the miracle, and by choice and will here is meant desire and wish; for the extraordinary act may be of a type not within the power and intention of the servant. Among the imams are those who distinguished between them: that every extraordinary event that occurs as a miracle for a prophet does not occur as a saintly wonder for a saint, such as reviving the dead, curing the blind and the leper, turning the staff into a snake, and splitting the sea into mountains. And the master asserts the prohibition of this and the prohibition of other extraordinary events at the hands of saints, and only permits what is in the way of answering a supplication, the appearance of water in the desert, and other things by which Allah honors His servants and which do not reach the level of extraordinary events.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition passage in which Sanusi clarifies three distinct types of extraordinary events: miracles (mu'jizat), magic (sihr), and saintly wonders (karamat). He begins by stating that through the earlier definition one can differentiate between a miracle and magic: magic has an ordinary cause that is consistently linked to it, whereas a miracle does not. He then cites Ibn 'Arafah's definition of magic as 'an extraordinary matter consistently linked to its cause,' and notes that al-Qarafi disagreed, considering magic not truly extraordinary but only seemingly so due to an obscure cause. Sanusi returns to the key distinction: a miracle is 'accompanying the claim of messengership,' while events that occur without a claim or with a claim other than prophethood (like sainthood) are not miracles. This leads to distinguishing miracles from saintly wonders: both are extraordinary, but only miracles are accompanied by the claim of prophethood; saintly wonders are not. He then presents two additional views from among the scholars on the difference: first, that a saintly wonder does not occur by the saint's choice or intent (where choice means desire and wish), while a miracle may; second, that certain miracles (like reviving the dead) are exclusive to prophets and never occur as saintly wonders. Finally, he mentions the position of 'the master' (al-Ustadh, likely al-Isfara'ini) who denies extraordinary events altogether at the hands of saints, except for inferior favors like answered supplications or providing water, which are not considered full-blown extraordinary events. The passage systematically refines the criteria for identifying a true miracle.

Key Terms
معجزة (mu'jizah)
سحر (sihr)
خارق للعادة (extraordinary)
سبب عادي (ordinary cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think magic is a truly extraordinary event without a natural cause, similar to a miracle.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that magic has an ordinary cause (سبب عادي) linked to it, whereas a miracle does not. Thus, magic is not a genuine miracle.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what distinguishes a miracle (mu'jizah) from magic (sihr)?
A) A miracle is more powerful than magic.
B) A miracle is always performed by a prophet, while magic is performed by sorcerers.
C) A miracle does not have an ordinary cause, while magic does.
D) A miracle is accompanied by a claim of sainthood.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference between a miracle and magic is that magic has an ordinary cause, while a miracle does not, and a miracle accompanies a claim to prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a remarkable event in your life was a direct miracle from Allah without stopping to consider if it might have an ordinary cause? How can this lesson refine your spiritual discernment?
?
The prophets’ miracles were clear proofs to their people. In your daily walk with Allah, what evidence do you rely on to confirm that you are following the truth? Are you seeking extraordinary signs, or are you content with the clear proofs of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The lesson warns that magic can look like a miracle due to hidden causes. What 'hidden causes' in your life—such as pride, status, or intellect—might lead you to mistake normal occurrences for divine favor? How can you cultivate a heart that attributes all good to Allah alone?

Miracles vs. Saintly Wonders: Three Scholarly Distinctions
Sanusi distinguishes miracles from saintly wonders using multiple scholarly criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three criteria used to distinguish miracles (mu'jizat) from saintly wonders (karamat), and the position of 'the master' limiting extraordinary events to prophets only.

Core Idea
Saintly wonders are extraordinary but never accompany a claim to prophethood.

Having distinguished miracles from magic, Sanusi now turns to the more subtle distinction between miracles and saintly wonders (karamat). He presents multiple scholarly views, including the criterion of accompaniment with prophethood, the saint's lack of choice, and the exclusivity of certain wonders to prophets. The passage also includes a stricter view that denies most extraordinary events to saints altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and translation carefully. Note that this section covers three to four different opinions on how to distinguish miracles from saintly wonders.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the primary distinction
Find the first and most fundamental criterion: accompaniment with the claim of prophethood. Understand that both miracles and saintly wonders are extraordinary, but only miracles are tied to the claim of prophethood.
This is the most widely accepted distinction.
3
Learn the second and third opinions
The second opinion states that saintly wonders do not occur by the saint's choice or intent, while miracles may. The third opinion says that certain specific wonders (like reviving the dead) are exclusive to prophets and never occur as saintly wonders.
Both opinions supplement the primary distinction.
4
Understand the stricter view ('the master')
The final view, attributed to 'al-Ustadh', denies that any genuine karamat reach the level of extraordinary events. It limits saintly wonders to minor favors like answered prayers or the appearance of water.
This view narrows the definition of karamah significantly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in your life or in the life of a pious person, do you immediately assume it is a sign of divine favor for yourself or them? How does this lesson's primary criterion—that miracles accompany a claim of prophethood—guard you from self-deception? Will you commit to testing such events against revelation, not your feelings?
?
The scholars differed on the nature of saintly wonders. Some denied that saints perform any genuine extraordinary acts. Have you ever been tempted to exalt a teacher or spiritual figure to a level that belongs only to prophets? What does this lesson teach you about keeping your heart from shirk in its subtle forms? Take a moment to repent if you have done so.
?
The strictest view limits saintly wonders to minor favors like answered prayers or water appearing in the desert. When your own prayer is answered, do you attribute it to your own piety or to Allah's sheer mercy? How can you train yourself to see answered prayers as a gift, not a reward for your worthiness, and to remain humble?

Belief in Resurrection: Definition and Proof
Sanusi defines al-hashr and al-nashr and gives a proof of resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-hashr and al-nashr and the rational proof that resurrection is true.

Core Idea
Resurrection is possible, and the truthful Prophet informed us of it—so it is true.

After establishing the truthfulness of Prophet Muhammad, Sanusi turns to the specific beliefs he brought, including the resurrection. In this passage, he defines the key terms and provides a concise proof that bodily revival after death is both possible and confirmed by divine revelation. This lesson unpacks both the definitions and the logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow from prophethood to resurrection.
Notice the list of eschatological items after the general obligation to believe.
2
Understand the context
Sanusi says that knowing the prior topics gives necessary knowledge of the Prophet's truthfulness, which demands belief in all he brought. This sets the stage for discussing resurrection.
3
Study the definitions of hash and nashr
Al-hashr is gathering and reviving bodies and driving them to the standing place. Al-nashr is reviving bodies after death. Note the focus on bodily revival.
The consensus of the milla followers is that Allah will revive bodies.
4
Grasp the proof
The proof: re-creation either means recreating substances after annihilation or assembling them after dispersal. Both are possible. Since the truthful one (the Prophet) reported it, resurrection is true.
This is a simple argument from possibility and report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على النيبنيات فإذا وُفِّقْتَ لعلم هذا كله حصل لكَ العلم ضرورةً بصدق رسالة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، فوجبَ الإيمانُ بهِ في كلِّ ما جاءَ بِهِ عَنِ اللَّهِ سبحانَهُ جملةً وتفصيلاً ؛ كالحشر والنشر ، والصراطِ والميزان ، والجنة والنار ، وعذاب القبر وسؤالِهِ (۳) الحشر : عبارةٌ عن جمع الأجسادِ وإحيائها وسَوْقِها إلى الموقفِ وغيره من مواطن الآخرةِ ، والنشرُ : عبارة عن إحيائها بعد مماتها . وأجمع المِلَّيُّونَ على أَنَّ اللهَ تعالى يحيي الأبدان بعد موتها ، والدليلُ عليهِ : أنَّ الإعادةَ : إِمَّا أنْ تكونَ بمعنى إعادة الجواهر بعد إعدامها ، أو بمعنى ضمها وجمعِها بعد تبديدِها ، وكلاهما ممكن ، وكلُّ ممكن أخبر الصادقُ بوقوعه فهو حق ، فالإعادة حق
Discussion of prophethood. So if you are granted knowledge of all this, you will necessarily attain knowledge of the truthfulness of the message of our Prophet and our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. So it becomes obligatory to believe in him in everything that he brought from Allah, Exalted is He, in general and in detail, such as al-hashr and al-nashr, the Straight Path (al-sirat) and the Balance (al-mizan), Paradise and Hellfire, and the punishment of the grave and its questioning (3). Al-hashr is an expression for the gathering of bodies and their revival and their driving to the standing place and other places of the Hereafter. Al-nashr is an expression for their revival after their death. And the milla followers (al-milliyyun) have agreed that Allah, Exalted is He, will revive bodies after their death. The proof for that is: re-creation (al-i'ada) is either in the sense of recreating the substances (jawahir) after their annihilation, or in the sense of assembling and gathering them after their dispersal. Both are possible. And every possible thing that the truthful one has informed of its occurrence is true. So recreation (al-i'ada) is true.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a conclusion from preceding discussions and a definition of resurrection with a proof. First, the author states that understanding the prior topics yields necessary knowledge of the Prophet's truthfulness, necessitating belief in all he brought, including eschatological matters. He then defines al-hashr as gathering and reviving bodies and driving them to the gathering place, and al-nashr as revival after death. He notes the consensus of the people of the milla that Allah will revive bodies. The proof is that recreation (al-i'ada) is either recreating substances after annihilation or assembling them after dispersal. Both meanings are possible. Since a truthful informant (the Prophet and ultimately Allah) reported its occurrence, it must be true. Thus, resurrection is established.

Key Terms
الحشر
النشر
الإعادة
الجواهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think resurrection is only spiritual, not bodily.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes bodily revival: al-hashr involves gathering and reviving bodies, and al-nashr is reviving them after death. The proof concerns the possibility of recreating or reassembling the physical body.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that resurrection is true according to Sanusi?
A) It is known by necessity.
B) It is possible and the truthful one reported it.
C) All people agree on it.
D) It is logically necessary.

Key Takeaway
Resurrection is true because it is both possible and reported by the truthful Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that you will be resurrected bodily tomorrow, which attachment in this world would you sever today?
?
The proof of resurrection rests on the truthfulness of the Prophet. Is there any unseen matter he brought that you have not fully accepted in your heart? What would it take to surrender that doubt completely?
?
You believe in resurrection intellectually, but does your daily schedule reflect a person who will stand before Allah? What would change if you lived with the resurrection already before your eyes?

The Second Meaning of Recreation: Definition and Rational Possibility
Sanusi defines the second meaning of recreation and distinguishes between scriptural hesitation and rational permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second meaning of recreation (i'ada) as the reassembly of parts and creation of life, and the distinction between scriptural hesitation on its mode (dispersion vs. non-existence) and intellectual permissibility.

Core Idea
Recreation is rationally permissible even if scripture leaves its exact manner uncertain.

Sanusi continues his discussion of recreation (al-i'ada) by clarifying its second meaning: the restoration of life to scattered parts. While the possibility of recreation is agreed upon, there is a subtle debate about whether it occurs from dispersion after death or from pure non-existence—a question that scripture prompts but reason leaves open. This passage distinguishes between what is known from revelation and what is rationally permissive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two aspects
Read the text and notice that first defines the second meaning of recreation, then introduces a hesitation (taraddud) about the mode (from dispersion or pure non-existence) based on scripture.
Sanusi says the possibility is 'obvious' (wadih) by consensus.
2
Distinguish scriptural hesitation from rational permissibility
Understand that the hesitation is only about the mode as indicated by scripture, not about rational possibility. Sanusi explicitly states that intellectually both modes are permissible and agreed upon.
This distinction is key: reason affirms possibility, revelation does not specify the mode.
3
Reflect on why this matters for kalam
Consider how this shows that kalam respects both reason and revelation: reason establishes general possibility, while revelation may leave details ambiguous.
This approach prevents overstepping rational inquiry into revealed specifics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إمكان الإعادة بالمعنى الثاني وهو جمع الأجزاء بعد تفريقها، وخلْقُ الحياة فيها - : فواضح، هذا إذا نظرنا إلى الإعادة إجماعاً، وفي كونه عن تفرق أو عدم محض تردُّد باعتبار ما دلّ عليه الشرع، أما الجواز العقلي فيهما فاتفاق، وفي إعادةِ الأعراض بأعيانها طريقان : الأولى : تُعادُ بأعيانِها باتفاق، والثانية : قولان، والصحيح منهما : إعادتها بأعيانها، وفي إعادةِ عين الوقتِ قولان، وكالصراط والميزان، وفي كون الموزونِ صحف الأعمال أو أجساماً تُخلَقُ أمثلة لها . . تردُّد، والجنَّةُ والنارُ وعذاب القبر وسؤاله )
As for the possibility of recreation in the second sense, which is the gathering of parts after their dispersion and the creation of life in them — it is obvious. This is when we consider recreation by consensus. And concerning whether it is from dispersion or pure non-existence, there is hesitation (taraddud) based on what the Sacred Law indicates. As for intellectual permissibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) of both, there is agreement. And concerning the recreation of the very same accidents (a'yan al-a'rad), there are two paths: the first: they are recreated in their very same selves by consensus. The second: there are two opinions, and the correct of them is that they are recreated in their very same selves. And concerning the recreation of the very same time, there are two opinions. And like the Straight Path and the Balance. And concerning whether what is weighed is the scrolls of deeds or bodies created as their likenesses, there is hesitation. And Paradise and Hellfire and the punishment of the grave and its questioning.)
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage discusses the second meaning of recreation (al-i'ada): reassembling dispersed parts and creating life. The author states this is obviously possible. He notes consensus on the possibility of recreation in general, but hesitation on whether it occurs from dispersion or from pure non-existence based on scripture. Intellectually, both are permissible. Then he covers the recreation of accidents (a'rad) themselves. There are two scholarly pathways: one affirming identity by consensus, and another with two opinions, the correct one being identity. He also mentions a debate on recreating the very time. He briefly references the Straight Path and the Balance, noting hesitation on whether what is weighed is the scrolls of deeds or created bodies as likenesses. The passage ends with a truncated enumeration of Paradise, Hellfire, punishment of the grave and its questioning, echoing earlier mentioned eschatological items.

Key Terms
الإعادة
المعنى الثاني
الجواز العقلي
التردد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hesitation about the mode means recreation is not possible.
Correction: The hesitation is only about whether it is from dispersion or non-existence, not about its possibility; reason affirms both.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the status of intellectual permissibility regarding recreation from dispersion or non-existence?
A) Hesitation
B) Agreement
C) Disagreement
D) Unclear

Key Takeaway
Recreation is rationally permissible regardless of its exact mode, which scripture leaves ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the resurrection of your own body—the same molecules that decay or disperse—does the hesitation Sanusi mentions about the exact mode ever trouble you? How does knowing that reason itself affirms the possibility of recreation change that hesitation?
?
Are you willing to trust that God's power is not limited by any mode, whether from complete nothingness or scattered dust? Reflect on an area of your life where you struggle to believe God can bring good out of apparent destruction. How does this lesson's emphasis on rational permissibility strengthen your reliance on divine power?

Recreation of Accidents, Time, and Eschatological Symbols
Sanusi reports consensus and debate on recreation of accidents, time, and the Balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly positions on the recreation of the very same accidents (a'yan al-a'rad), the recreation of time, and the hesitation about what is weighed on the Balance.

Core Idea
The correct view is that accidents are recreated identically, and time's recreation is debated.

Having established the possibility of bodily recreation, Sanusi turns to more specific elements: the accidents (a'rad) that inhere in bodies, the very time (waqt) of existence, and the eschatological symbols of the Straight Path and Balance. He reports a consensus that accidents are recreated in their very same selves, with a minority opinion, and a parallel debate about time. He also notes hesitation about whether what is weighed on the Balance is the scrolls of deeds or created bodies as representations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two 'paths' regarding accidents
Sanusi mentions two scholarly paths (tariqan) for the recreation of accidents. The first path is a consensus (ittifaq) that they are recreated identically. The second path has two opinions, with the correct one being identity.
Note the technical term 'a'yan al-a'rad' (the very same accidents).
2
Observe the debate on recreation of time
Sanusi states there are two opinions on whether the very time itself is recreated. He does not specify which is correct, indicating a recognized difference.
This parallels the debate on accidents.
3
Understand the reference to the Straight Path and Balance
Sanusi says 'and like the Straight Path and the Balance' (وكالصراط والميزان), meaning similar debates apply to these eschatological realities.
This is a brief reference, not an elaboration.
4
Consider the hesitation on what is weighed
Sanusi notes hesitation (taraddud) about whether what is weighed is the scrolls of deeds (suhuf al-a'mal) or bodies created as their likenesses.
This shows that the exact nature of weighing is not definitively known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إمكان الإعادة بالمعنى الثاني وهو جمع الأجزاء بعد تفريقها، وخلْقُ الحياة فيها - : فواضح، هذا إذا نظرنا إلى الإعادة إجماعاً، وفي كونه عن تفرق أو عدم محض تردُّد باعتبار ما دلّ عليه الشرع، أما الجواز العقلي فيهما فاتفاق، وفي إعادةِ الأعراض بأعيانها طريقان : الأولى : تُعادُ بأعيانِها باتفاق، والثانية : قولان، والصحيح منهما : إعادتها بأعيانها، وفي إعادةِ عين الوقتِ قولان، وكالصراط والميزان، وفي كون الموزونِ صحف الأعمال أو أجساماً تُخلَقُ أمثلة لها . . تردُّد، والجنَّةُ والنارُ وعذاب القبر وسؤاله )
As for the possibility of recreation in the second sense, which is the gathering of parts after their dispersion and the creation of life in them — it is obvious. This is when we consider recreation by consensus. And concerning whether it is from dispersion or pure non-existence, there is hesitation (taraddud) based on what the Sacred Law indicates. As for intellectual permissibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) of both, there is agreement. And concerning the recreation of the very same accidents (a'yan al-a'rad), there are two paths: the first: they are recreated in their very same selves by consensus. The second: there are two opinions, and the correct of them is that they are recreated in their very same selves. And concerning the recreation of the very same time, there are two opinions. And like the Straight Path and the Balance. And concerning whether what is weighed is the scrolls of deeds or bodies created as their likenesses, there is hesitation. And Paradise and Hellfire and the punishment of the grave and its questioning.)

Commentary
This passage discusses the second meaning of recreation (al-i'ada): reassembling dispersed parts and creating life. The author states this is obviously possible. He notes consensus on the possibility of recreation in general, but hesitation on whether it occurs from dispersion or from pure non-existence based on scripture. Intellectually, both are permissible. Then he covers the recreation of accidents (a'rad) themselves. There are two scholarly pathways: one affirming identity by consensus, and another with two opinions, the correct one being identity. He also mentions a debate on recreating the very time. The passage ends with a truncated enumeration of Paradise, Hellfire, punishment of the grave and its questioning, echoing earlier mentioned eschatological items.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are not recreated identically.
Correction: Sanusi reports consensus on identity for the first path and the correct opinion for the second path is identity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct opinion regarding the re-creation of accidents?
A) They are not recreated.
B) They are recreated in their very same selves.
C) They are recreated in different forms.

Key Takeaway
The prevailing view is that accidents are recreated identically, while the recreation of time is debated, and what is weighed on the Balance remains a matter of hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the correct view is that accidents are recreated identically. Does your daily life reflect a lived conviction that every action you commit is exactly preserved and will return to you? How would you change if you truly believed your deeds are physically weighed?
?
The passage reports hesitation among scholars about what exactly is weighed on the Balance — scrolls or created bodies. Does this uncertainty unsettle you, or does it make the reality of judgment more tangible? How will you prepare for a Day when your deeds might take a visible form?
?

Resurrection: Definition and Proof from Possibility
Defines al-hashr and al-nashr, states consensus, and gives a syllogistic proof for resurrection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-Hashr and al-Nashr, the unanimous agreement on resurrection, and the logical proof based on possibility and the truthful report.

Core Idea
Resurrection is rationally possible and divinely promised.

This lesson introduces two key eschatological terms: al-Hashr (gathering) and al-Nashr (reviving). After defining these, the author presents a concise proof that resurrection is not merely a revealed doctrine but something rationally possible, affirmed by the report of the Truthful (the Prophet).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the definitions
Notice that the passage defines two terms: al-Hashr and al-Nashr. Pay attention to the distinct actions described for each.
Al-Hashr involves gathering and driving to the standing place; al-Nashr is simply reviving after death.
3
Examine the proof
The proof is a syllogism. Find the two possibilities for i'ada (return) and see how they lead to the conclusion that resurrection is true.
The proof relies on rational possibility and the reliability of the truthful informant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحشر : عبارة عن جمع الأجساد وإحيائها وسوقها إلى الموقف وغيره من مواطن الآخرة ، والنشر : عبارة عن إحيائها بعد مماتها . وأجمع المليون على أن الله تعالى يحيي الأبدان بعد موتها ، والدليل عليه : أن الإعادة : إما أن تكون بمعنى إعادة الجواهر بعد إعدامها ، أو بمعنى ضمها وجمعها بعد تبديدها ، وكلاهما ممكن ، وكل ممكن أخبر الصادق بوقوعه فهو حق ، فالإعادة حق
Al-Hashr is an expression for gathering the bodies, reviving them, and driving them to the standing place and other stations of the Hereafter. And al-Nashr is an expression for reviving them after their death. And the people of the religion have agreed that Allah Most High will revive the bodies after their death. The proof for that is: that the return (al-i'ada) is either in the sense of recreating the substances after their annihilation, or in the sense of joining and gathering them after their dispersal, and both are possible. And every possible thing that the Truthful One has reported its occurrence is true, so the return is true.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of two key terms related to the Resurrection: al-Hashr (gathering) and al-Nashr (reviving). It then states the consensus of the Muslim community (al-milliyyun) that Allah will indeed revive bodies after death. The proof given is a logical syllogism: the return (i'ada) can be understood in two ways (recreating substances from nothing or reassembling dispersed parts), both of which are possible. Since the truthful (i.e., the Prophet) has reported that it will occur, and whatever possible is reported is true, the resurrection is necessarily true.

Key Terms
الحشر
النشر
الإعادة
الجواهر
الصادق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof for resurrection is purely scriptural and does not involve rational reasoning.
Correction: The passage shows that the proof includes a rational argument: the return is possible in both senses, and the truthful report transforms possibility into certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the proof for resurrection given in this passage?
A) Consensus of the community
B) Possibility reported by the truthful
C) Direct sensory evidence
D) Philosophical necessity

Key Takeaway
The resurrection is a rationally possible event affirmed by divine report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that resurrection is both possible and promised. How does the certainty of standing before Allah tomorrow affect a decision you are making today? What will you change right now to face that moment with hope rather than regret?
?
The passage argues that whatever is possible and reported by the Truthful is true. Do you truly live as if the events of the Hereafter are more certain than your next breath? What is one example from your daily routine that reveals a lack of that conviction?
?
You believe resurrection will happen. But have you prepared for it? What is one specific sin you have been tolerating because deep down you think there is no real accountability? How will you break that habit starting today?

The Possibility and Nature of the Return (I'ada)
Enumerates views on reassembling bodies and recreating accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold meanings of i'ada (reassembling bodies vs. recreating accidents) and the spectrum of scholarly consensus and debate on these eschatological matters.

Core Idea
The return is intellectually possible by consensus; only its mode is debated, settled by revelation.

Sanusi turns to the second sense of 'return' (i'ada): the reassembling of bodily parts after death. He asserts its obvious possibility but notes that the exact modality—whether from dispersal or absolute non-existence—is determined by revelation, not mere reason. The passage then surveys different opinions on recreating accidents, time, and entities like the Balance, grounding the discussion in both intellectual agreement and scriptural nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the terminology sink in.
Pay attention to the terms 'إعادة' and 'أعراض'.
2
Distinguish the two senses of i'ada
The passage first defines i'ada as gathering parts and creating life. Separate this from the sense of recreating accidents. Note the consensus on intellectual possibility.
3
Identify the two ways concerning accidents
The passage presents two 'ways' (طريقان). The first is a consensus, the second has two opinions. Identify which opinion is deemed correct.
The correct opinion: accidents are recreated identically.
4
Recognize the debated matters
Note the matters where there are two opinions (time, Bridge, Balance) and the hesitation about what is weighed (deeds or bodies). End with Paradise, Hell, grave torment.
These are all part of eschatological realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إمكان الإعادة بالمعنى الثاني وهو جمع الأجزاء بعد تفريقها وخلق الحياة فيها : فواضح ، هذا إذا نظرنا إلى الإعادة إجماعاً ، وفي كونه عن تفرق أو عدم محض تردد باعتبار ما دل عليه الشرع ، أما الجواز العقلي فيهما فاتفاق ، وفي إعادة الأعراض بأعيانها طريقان : الأولى : تعاد بأعيانها باتفاق ، والثانية : قولان ، والصحيح منهما : إعادتها بأعيانها ، وفي إعادة عين الوقت قولان ، وكالصراط والميزان ، وفي كون الموزون صحف الأعمال أو أجساماً تخلق أمثلة لها تردد ، والجنة والنار وعذاب القبر وسؤاله)
As for the possibility of the return in the second sense, which is the gathering of the parts after their dispersal and the creation of life in them, it is obvious. This is when we consider the return by consensus. And regarding whether it is from dispersal or absolute non-existence, there is hesitation as per what the Law indicates. As for intellectual permissibility in both, there is agreement. And regarding the recreation of accidents as they are, there are two ways: the first is that they are recreated as they are by consensus; the second: two opinions, and the correct of them is that they are recreated as they are. And regarding the recreation of the identical time, two opinions. And similarly the Bridge (al-Sirat) and the Balance (al-Mizan). And regarding whether the weighed thing is the scrolls of deeds or bodies created as examples of them, there is hesitation. And Paradise, Hellfire, the torment of the grave, and its questioning.)
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility of the second meaning of i'ada, which is reassembling the parts of the body after they have dispersed and then creating life in them. The author states that this is obviously possible. He then notes that there is consensus on the general possibility of recreation, but there is hesitation regarding whether the original creation was from absolute non-existence or from dispersal, a matter determined by revelation. Intellectually, both are permissible. The passage then addresses the recreation of accidents (a'rad): there is consensus that they are recreated as they are in one sense, but there are two opinions on the other, with the correct view being that they are recreated identically. Similarly, the recreation of the identical time is debated. Other matters like the Bridge (al-Sirat) and the Balance (al-Mizan) are mentioned, along with the question of what is weighed. The passage ends with a mention of Paradise, Hellfire, the torment of the grave, and its questioning.

Key Terms
الإعادة بالمعنى الثاني
جمع الأجزاء
إعادة الأعراض
الصراط
الميزان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual possibility alone is sufficient to determine the nature of the resurrection; revelation is unnecessary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while intellectual permissibility of both modes is agreed upon, the specific modality (from dispersal or non-existence) is based on revelation, not reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct opinion regarding the recreation of accidents (a'rad) in the second way?
A) They are not recreated
B) They are recreated identically (bi a'yaniha)
C) They are recreated as new examples
D) There is no consensus

Key Takeaway
The resurrection of bodies is intellectually possible by consensus, but the mode and the re-creation of accidents, time, and eschatological objects are settled by revelation, with some debates remaining.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live today with the same certainty that the resurrection will happen as you have about the sunrise tomorrow? If not, what specific truth from this lesson will you internalize to change that—starting now?
?
Your scroll of deeds—or a body created as its example—will be weighed in the Balance. Are you actively accumulating good deeds that you would be proud to see weighed, or are you neglecting what this lesson teaches about the reality of that accounting?
?
The mode of resurrection is settled by revelation, not your intellect. Is there an aspect of the afterlife that you have been rationalizing away or treating as metaphorical simply because it doesn't fit your assumptions? Will you now submit to what Allah has revealed, even if your mind struggles with it?

No Impossibility from Receiver or Agent: Quranic Proof
Argues that neither the bodies (receiver) nor God (agent) pose any obstacle to resurrection, citing Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic argument from Surah Ya-Sin demonstrates that neither the bodies (receiver) nor God (agent) present any rational obstacle to resurrection.

Core Idea
The first creation proves the body's receptivity; God's power proves the agent's capability.

Sanusi now addresses a common doubt about resurrection: that the disintegration of bodies makes them impossible to recreate. He responds by examining both the receiver (the body) and the agent (God). The Quran itself provides the proof, showing that the very fact of initial creation establishes the body's possibility, while God's attributes of power and knowledge remove any obstacle from His side. Let us see how the verse from Surah Ya-Sin is unpacked.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: first logical reasoning, then Quranic citation.
2
Identify the two negations
The passage negates impossibility from two sides: the receiver (القابل) and the agent (الفاعل). Find how each is negated in the explanation.
3
Connect to the Quranic proof
Notice how each part of the verse (أنشأها أول مرة and الخلاق العليم) directly addresses one of the two potential obstacles. Reflect on the use of intensive forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحسب قابلها ، وإن نظرنا إليها بحسب فاعلها وهو الله جل وعلا فلا خفاء أن قدرته لا يتعاصى عليها ممكن ، وعلمه محيط بكل شيء ، فلا تعذر إذاً لا من جهة القابل ، ولا من جهة الفاعل . وإلى نفي التعذرين أشار القرآن في قوله تعالى : ﴿ قَالَ مَن يُحْيِي الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ * قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [ يس : 78-79 ] فنفى التعذر من جهة المعاد القابل بقوله : أنشأها أول مرة أي : ذاته قابلة للوجود بدليل النشأة الأولى ، ويستحيل أن تنقلب الحقيقة من إمكان شيء إلى استحالته ، ونفى التعذر من جهة الفاعل بقوله : وهو الخلاق العليم بصيغتي المبالغة وبقوله : أنشأها )
According to its receiver (the subject), and if we look at it according to its agent (al-Fa'il), which is Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, then there is no doubt that His power is not incapable of any possible thing, and His knowledge encompasses everything. So there is no impossibility from the side of the receiver nor from the side of the agent. And the Quran indicated the negation of the two impossibilities in His statement: 'He said: Who shall give life to the bones when they are decayed? Say: He shall give life to them Who brought them into existence at first, and He is Knower of all creation' (Ya-Sin 78-79). So He negated the impossibility from the side of the resurrected receiver by His saying 'Who brought them into existence at first' meaning: its essence is receptive to existence by the evidence of the first creation, and it is impossible for the reality to change from the possibility of something to its impossibility. And He negated the impossibility from the side of the agent by His saying 'and He is the Great Creator, the Knower' with two intensive forms, and by His saying 'brought them into existence'.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage explains that there is no obstacle to resurrection either from the side of the bodies (the receiver) or from the side of God (the agent). For the receiver, the very fact that they were created once proves that they are receptive to existence. For the agent, God's power and knowledge are perfect. The Quranic verse from Surah Ya-Sin is cited to support both: the phrase 'Who brought them into existence at first' shows the receiver's capability, and 'He is the Great Creator, the All-Knowing' shows the agent's capability. It is impossible for a thing's essential nature to change from possibility to impossibility.

Key Terms
القابل
الفاعل
التعذر
النشأة الأولى
الخلاق العليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that once a body disintegrates, it becomes impossible to recreate.
Correction: The passage argues that the first creation proves the body's receptivity; its essence remains possible. God's power is not limited by the state of the matter.

Checkpoint
How does the Quranic phrase 'أنشأها أول مرة' negate one of the impossibilities?
A) It shows that God is powerful enough to recreate.
B) It shows that the body is receptive to existence because it was created before.

D) It shows that the resurrection is a natural process.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic proof from Surah Ya-Sin establishes that resurrection is not impossible either from the side of the body (receiver) or from the side of God (agent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God’s power faces no impossibility, what excuse do you have for not trusting Him with the parts of your life that feel dead — relationships, habits, hopes? Will you let the first creation of those things remind you that renewal is always possible?

Refutation of Two Doubts About Resurrection
Presents two deniers' doubts and gives Quranic answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main doubts raised by deniers of bodily resurrection and how the Quran answers each one.

Core Idea
God's complete knowledge and power make resurrection, even from dust, entirely possible.

This passage addresses two common arguments used to deny bodily resurrection: the scattering of body parts and the change of nature after death. Sanusi presents each doubt and then shows how the Quran provides clear and powerful responses, affirming that Allah's knowledge and power overcome all apparent impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The passage uses concise language; focus on the structure: doubt followed by answer.

The answer relies on Allah's knowledge and power.
3
Identify the second doubt and its answer
The second doubt involves the change of nature from life to dust. What Quranic example is used to refute it?
The example of fire from green wood shows Allah can bring opposites together.
4

Consider how both answers point to Allah's attributes: knowledge, power, and creative ability. Why does this refute the doubt?

Commentary
This passage presents two common doubts of those who deny bodily resurrection and the answers from the Quran. The first doubt is that it seems improbable for the scattered parts of the body to be gathered precisely after mixing with other matter. The answer is that God's knowledge encompasses all parts and His power can recompose them. The second doubt is that when the body becomes dust, its nature (life) is lost, as life requires heat and moisture. The Quranic answer is that God can create fire from a green tree, showing that He can bring opposites together and create life from dust.

Proof of the Possibility of Resurrection from the Quran
Sanusi argues for the possibility of bodily resurrection by considering the recipient and the agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses rational analysis and Quranic evidence to prove that bodily resurrection is possible by considering the recipient (the body) and the agent (God).

Core Idea
Resurrection is possible because the body can be remade and God's power is limitless.

Sanusi presents a proof for the possibility of bodily resurrection that combines rational reasoning with Quranic evidence. He argues from two perspectives: the capacity of the body to be recreated, and the infinite power and knowledge of God. The Quranic verses from Surah Ya-Sin are cited to support each aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi structures his argument around two angles: the recipient (قابل) and the agent (فاعل).
The passage is dense but logical.
2
Understand the two aspects
Identify the two sources of impossibility that Sanusi negates: one related to the body's capacity and one related to God's power. See how he uses the Quran to negate both.
The first creation proves possibility; God's attributes prove ability.
3
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
Study the verses cited (Ya-Sin 78-79, 81). See how each part of the verse addresses one aspect. The phrase 'who produced them the first time' shows possibility; 'He is the Creator, the Knowing' shows capability.
Sanusi uses the intensive forms to emphasize.
4
Ask yourself the key takeaway
Why is this proof important for Islamic theology? It defends resurrection against those who deem it impossible.
This connects to the broader topic of samiyyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحسب قابلها ، وإن نظرنا إليها بحسَب فاعلها وهو الله جل وعلا فلا خفاء أنَّ قدرته لا يتعاصى عليها ممكن ، وعلمه محيط بكلِّ شيءٍ ، فلا تعدُّرَ إذاً لا مِنْ جهةِ القابل ، ولا مِنْ جهة الفاعل وإلى نفي التعذرين أشار القرآن في قوله تعالى : ﴿قَالَ مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ * قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ ) يسَ : ۷۸-۷۹] ، فنفى التعدُّرَ مِنْ جَهةِ المُعادِ القابل بقولِهِ : أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ ) أي : ذاتُهُ قابلة للوجودِ بدليل النشأة الأولى ، ويستحيلُ أنْ تنقلب الحقيقةُ مِنْ إمكانِ شيءٍ إلى استحالتِهِ ، ونفى التعدُّرَ مِنْ جهةِ الفاعل بقولِهِ : ﴿ وَهُوَ الْخَلَّقُ الْعَلِيمُ ﴾ [يس : ۸۱] بصيغتي المبالغة وبقوله : أَنشَأَهَا )

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of bodily resurrection. The author argues by considering two aspects: the recipient (the body) and the agent (God). For the recipient, the fact that it existed once shows it is possible; possibility does not change to impossibility. For the agent, God's power and knowledge are infinite, so no possible thing is beyond Him. The Quranic verses are cited as evidence. The passage follows the order: first states the general principle, then cites the Quran that negates both impossibilities, then explains each.

Sanusi presents the first doubt and its refutation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and power make resurrection certain, not improbable.

Sanusi begins by presenting the first doubt raised by deniers of bodily resurrection: that it seems impossible to reassemble scattered particles back to their original form. He then refutes this using a straightforward argument from divine attributes and a Quranic verse.
